The Principle of Eternity and Argument from Change
Sanusi states the principle that the eternal cannot cease, then argues from change to prove the world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the principle that the eternal cannot cease to argue that the world must be temporal because its attributes change.

Core Idea
Change proves temporality; the eternal never changes.

In this passage, Sanusi lays down a fundamental principle of kalam: anything proven to be eternal cannot cease to exist. He then applies this principle to the observable world, using the fact that the world's attributes are constantly changing to demonstrate that these attributes—and therefore the world itself—must be temporal, not eternal. This is a classic argument for the world's createdness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow from principle to application.
Notice the concise style; every phrase is carefully chosen.
2
Grasp the principle: the eternal cannot cease
Sanusi states that if something is established as eternal (qadim), its non-existence is impossible. This is a rational axiom.
This principle is foundational for many kalam arguments.
3
Examine the argument from change
He asks you to look at the change in the world's attributes—they are acquired and occur. This change indicates that they are temporal, because if they were eternal they could not change.
Change includes both coming into existence and ceasing.
4
Connect attributes to the world's essence
Since the attributes are temporal, the subject (the world itself) must also be temporal, because the world cannot exist without its changing attributes. This completes the proof that the world is hadith (temporal).
This step relies on the impossibility of a subject being devoid of its attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمُهُ وأيضاً : لو نظرت إلى تغير صفات العالم قبولاً وحصولاً لدلَّكَ ذلكَ على حدوثها؛ لِمَا يأتي من استحالة تغيرِ القديم، ودلَّ حدوثها على حدوثِ موصوفها؛ لاستحالة [عرو؟] عنها.
What has been established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible. And also: if you look at the change of the attributes of the world in terms of acceptance and occurrence, that would indicate to you their temporality, due to what will come about the impossibility of change of the eternal; and their temporality indicates the temporality of their subject, due to the impossibility of the subject being devoid of them.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage contains two connected statements. First, Sanusi states a principle: anything proven to be eternal cannot cease to exist. Second, he provides an argument from the observable change in the attributes of the world. The attributes of the world undergo change—they are acquired and occur in time. This change shows that these attributes are temporal (haditha), because if they were eternal, they could not change. Then, since the attributes are temporal, the subject (the world itself) must also be temporal, because it cannot exist without its attributes. This is a standard kalam argument for the world's temporality.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
حدوث (huduth)
تغير (taghayyur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that change in the world only proves temporality of the world's parts, not the whole.
Correction: The argument relies on the world's subject being inseparable from its changing attributes; thus the whole world is temporal.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's argument, what does the change in the world's attributes indicate?
A) The world is eternal.
B) The world's attributes are eternal.
C) The attributes are temporal and therefore the world is temporal.
D) The attributes are eternal but the world is temporal.

Key Takeaway
The eternal is immutable, so the world's change proves it is temporal, and its attributes' temporality proves the world itself is temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely daily on things that change—your health, your money, your relationships. If these are all temporal, where is your ultimate security? Are you actually building your life on what is eternal, or are you trusting in fleeting shadows? What concrete change can you make today to shift your trust from the temporal to the eternal?
?
If the eternal never changes, and God is eternal, then His attributes are unchanging. How does this confront your moments when you feel God has changed toward you—when you feel His mercy has withdrawn or His anger has increased? Are you projecting human variability onto the unchanging Lord? What would it mean for you to truly believe that His mercy is as constant as His eternity?
?
The argument from change demolishes any illusion that the world is self-sufficient or permanent. Look at your schedule, your worries, your ambitions—do they reflect a deep awareness that this world is passing away, or do you live as if it will last forever? If you truly internalized that everything around you is temporal, what would you stop worrying about, and what would you start pursuing right now?

First Argument: Impossibility of Infinite Number
Sanusi presents the first argument against infinite regress: impossibility of an actual infinite number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite number of past events is logically impossible and how this refutes an infinite temporal regress.

Core Idea
An actual infinite number is impossible because it lacks a beginning to end.

Sanusi begins his refutation by showing that if events had no beginning, there would be an infinite number of events before the present. But any number must have two ends, and an infinite number cannot be traversed. This leads to a contradiction, proving the assumption false.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: فراغ, عدد, طرفين.
The passage is a logical proof, so pay attention to each step.
2
Understand the logical sequence
The argument: assumption of no beginning → infinite void before present → void of number requires two ends → infinite void has no end → impossible → therefore the assumption is false.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The present events exist, but they depend on an impossible condition. Hence the assumption of infinite past is contradictory and must be rejected.
This is a cornerstone of the kalam cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقديرها حوادث لا مبدأ لها يؤدي إلى فراغ ما لا نهاية له عدداً قبل ما وُجِدَ منها الآنَ، لكن فراغ العددِ يستلزم انتهاءَ طرفيهِ، ففراغ ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنْ عدد الحوادث محال، فما توقَّفَ عليهِ مِنْ وجودِ الحوادثِ الآن يجبُ أنْ يكون محالاً، فيلزم أن تكونَ عدماً مع تحقق وجودها. وأيضاً : يلزم على وجودِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها أنْ يقارنَ الوجود الأزليَّ عدمُهُ وأن يستحيل عندَ تطبيق ما فرغ منها بدون زيادة على نفسِهِ معَ زيادة .. ما عُلِمَ بينَ العددينِ مِنْ وجوب المساواة أو نقيضها. وأن يصح في كلِّ حادث ثبوت حكم بفراغ ما لا نهاية له قبلَهُ وهكذا لا إلى أوَّل في الأحكام ، ومِنْ لازمها سبقُ محكوم عليه بالفراغ، فيلزم أن يسبق أزلي أزليّاً ، وإنْ أُجيبَ بالنهايةِ في الأحكام لزمَ أنَّ ما يتناهى لا يتناهى بزيادة واحد !
And positing that events have no beginning leads to a void of infinite number before what exists now from them, but the void of number necessitates the termination of its two ends, so a void of infinite number of events is impossible. So what the existence of events now depends on must be impossible, so it would follow that they are non-existent despite their existence being verified. And also: it follows from the existence of events with no first that the eternal existence would be accompanied by its non-existence, and that when applying what is void of them without increase upon itself with increase, what is known between the two numbers of necessity of equality or its opposite, and that in every event it is possible to establish a judgment by a void infinite before it, and so on without a first in judgments, and from its necessities is the precedence of a ruled-upon by a void. So it would follow that an eternal precedes an eternal. And if it is answered by finitude in judgments, then it would follow that what is finite is not finite by the addition of one.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against an infinite regress of temporal events. First, Sanusi argues that if events have no beginning, then before any given event there would be an infinite number of prior events. This would imply an actual infinite number, which is impossible because any number must have two ends (a beginning and an end). An infinite number with no beginning cannot be traversed. Therefore, the assumption that events have no beginning leads to a contradiction: the existence of the present events depends on an impossibility, so those events would have to be non-existent, yet they exist. Second, Sanusi gives a further argument: If there are events with no first, then the eternal (God) would be contemporaneous with non-existence, which is absurd. Also, when comparing the infinite series to itself, it leads to a contradiction of equality and inequality. Finally, if we consider judgments (or decrees) that are based on infinite prior events, we would have an infinite chain of judgments, leading to an eternal preceding another eternal, which is impossible. If one tries to avoid this by positing finitude in judgments, then the chain of events would be finite, contradicting the original assumption.

Key Terms
فراغ (farāgh)
عدد (ʿadad)
طرفين (tarafayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think infinite regress in the past is possible as an actual infinite series.
Correction: Sanusi shows that an actual infinite series is impossible because it lacks a first term, making it a logical contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against infinite regress presented by Sanusi?
A) It leads to a void of infinite number, which is impossible.
B) It requires an infinite number of gods.
C) It makes God temporal.
D) It denies causality.

Key Takeaway
An infinite number of past events is impossible because it would create an unending void that cannot be traversed, proving infinite regress false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi proves that an infinite past would make your present existence impossible. Yet you are here, reading this. What does that demand you accept about the ultimate Cause of your very breath?
?
If you truly believe that the chain of events ends with a Necessary Being, why do you still live as if your next moment depends on your own planning rather than on His constant creation?
?
This argument strips you of any excuse to avoid accountability: your existence is not self- sufficient. Will you thank the One who brought you into being, or will you turn away as if you created yourself?

Second Argument: Absurdities from Infinite Regress
Sanusi presents three additional absurdities resulting from infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional logical absurdities that arise from assuming an infinite regress of events, including contradictions in comparing infinite sets and an eternal preceding another eternal.

Core Idea
Infinite regress leads to contradictions like equality vs. inequality and an eternal preceding another eternal.

Sanusi provides further arguments showing that an infinite regress of events leads to multiple absurdities. He mentions three: (1) the eternal would coexist with non-existence, (2) comparing infinite sets leads to contradictions of equality and inequality, and (3) an infinite chain of judgments would force an eternal to precede another eternal, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the second passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the three sub-arguments introduced by 'أن يقارن...' and 'وأن يستحيل...' and 'وأن يصح...'.
The passage is dense; take time to parse each part.
2
Identify the three absurdities
First absurdity: eternal existence accompanied by non-existence. Second: comparing the void of events without increase with itself with increase leads to contradiction. Third: infinite chain of judgments implies an eternal preceding another eternal.
3
Understand the final dilemma
If you try to avoid infinite judgments by positing finitude, then the events themselves become finite, which contradicts the original assumption.
This is a strong refutation of any attempt to avoid the first argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقديرها حوادث لا مبدأ لها يؤدي إلى فراغ ما لا نهاية له عدداً قبل ما وُجِدَ منها الآنَ، لكن فراغ العددِ يستلزم انتهاءَ طرفيهِ، ففراغ ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنْ عدد الحوادث محال، فما توقَّفَ عليهِ مِنْ وجودِ الحوادثِ الآن يجبُ أنْ يكون محالاً، فيلزم أن تكونَ عدماً مع تحقق وجودها. وأيضاً : يلزم على وجودِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها أنْ يقارنَ الوجود الأزليَّ عدمُهُ وأن يستحيل عندَ تطبيق ما فرغ منها بدون زيادة على نفسِهِ معَ زيادة .. ما عُلِمَ بينَ العددينِ مِنْ وجوب المساواة أو نقيضها. وأن يصح في كلِّ حادث ثبوت حكم بفراغ ما لا نهاية له قبلَهُ وهكذا لا إلى أوَّل في الأحكام ، ومِنْ لازمها سبقُ محكوم عليه بالفراغ، فيلزم أن يسبق أزلي أزليّاً ، وإنْ أُجيبَ بالنهايةِ في الأحكام لزمَ أنَّ ما يتناهى لا يتناهى بزيادة واحد !
And positing that events have no beginning leads to a void of infinite number before what exists now from them, but the void of number necessitates the termination of its two ends, so a void of infinite number of events is impossible. So what the existence of events now depends on must be impossible, so it would follow that they are non-existent despite their existence being verified. And also: it follows from the existence of events with no first that the eternal existence would be accompanied by its non-existence, and that when applying what is void of them without increase upon itself with increase, what is known between the two numbers of necessity of equality or its opposite, and that in every event it is possible to establish a judgment by a void infinite before it, and so on without a first in judgments, and from its necessities is the precedence of a ruled-upon by a void. So it would follow that an eternal precedes an eternal. And if it is answered by finitude in judgments, then it would follow that what is finite is not finite by the addition of one.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against an infinite regress of temporal events. First, Sanusi argues that if events have no beginning, then before any given event there would be an infinite number of prior events. This would imply an actual infinite number, which is impossible because any number must have two ends (a beginning and an end). An infinite number with no beginning cannot be traversed. Therefore, the assumption that events have no beginning leads to a contradiction: the existence of the present events depends on an impossibility, so those events would have to be non-existent, yet they exist. Second, Sanusi gives a further argument: If there are events with no first, then the eternal (God) would be contemporaneous with non-existence, which is absurd. Also, when comparing the infinite series to itself, it leads to a contradiction of equality and inequality. Finally, if we consider judgments (or decrees) that are based on infinite prior events, we would have an infinite chain of judgments, leading to an eternal preceding another eternal, which is impossible. If one tries to avoid this by positing finitude in judgments, then the chain of events would be finite, contradicting the original assumption.

Key Terms
أزلي (azali)
حكم (ḥukm)
تطبيق (taṭbīq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think infinite regress only leads to a harmless infinite series.
Correction: Sanusi shows it leads to actual contradictions such as equality/inequality and an eternal preceding an eternal.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the absurdities Sanusi mentions in the second argument?
A) The eternal would be accompanied by non-existence.
B) Comparing infinite sets leads to contradiction.
C) An infinite chain of judgments necessitates an eternal preceding an eternal.
D) The existence of God is negated.

Key Takeaway
The assumption of infinite past events leads to multiple logical absurdities, decisively refuting the possibility of infinite temporal regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that infinite regress leads to contradictions. Have you ever felt overwhelmed by a chain of causes in your own life—like bad habits or sins that seem to have no beginning? How does the necessity of a first cause force you to confront the starting point of your actions and your accountability before the Eternal? What is that starting point for you personally, and are you willing to change it today?
?
If infinite regress is impossible, then your existence has a definite beginning in time. Does this make you feel a sense of fragility or urgency? How should the fact that you are not eternal affect the way you prioritize your relationship with the One who is eternal? What keeps you from living with that urgency right now?

Three Views on How Miracles Indicate Truthfulness
Sanusi presents three views and provides proof for the first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proposed types of indication (rational, conventional, habitual) and why the first two are considered impossible for a liar.

Core Idea
Miracles cannot come from a liar because that would contradict reason or God's promise.

Sanusi begins by posing the core question: how does a miracle show that a prophet is truthful? He presents three possible answers: rational, conventional, or habitual. For the first two, he argues that a miracle performed by a liar is impossible, as it would either break a rational proof or break God's own convention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three views
The passage presents three views on how miracles indicate truth: rational (ʿaqliyya), conventional (waḍʿiyya), and habitual (ʿādiyya). Find where each is mentioned.
3
Understand the proof for the first two
Sanusi argues that for the rational and conventional views, it is impossible for a miracle to come from a liar. Trace the reasoning: rational would contradict reason, conventional would break God's promise.
The proof for the conventional view relies on God's inner speech always being true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل دَلالةُ المعجزة على صدق الرسولِ دَلالة عقلية أو وضعيّةٌ ، أو عاديَّةٌ بحسَبِ القرائنِ ؟ أقوال أما على الأوَّلين فيستحيلُ صدورها على يد الكاذب ; لما يلزم على الأَوَّلِ مِنْ نقضِ الدليل العقلي ، وعلى الثاني مِنَ الخُلْفِ في خبرِهِ جلَّ وعلا ; إذْ تصديق الكاذب كذب ، والكذب عليه جلَّ وعلا محالٌ ; لأنَّ خبرَهُ على وَفْق علمِهِ ، فيكون صدقاً ، فلو انتفى لانتفى العلم ملزومه ، وهو محال ; لما عرفتَ مِنْ وجوبِهِ فإن قلت : قد وجدنا العالِمَ منا بالشيء يخبرُ عنه بالكذب . قلنا : كلامنا في الخبر النفسي ، لا في الألفاظ ; لاستحالة اتصاف الباري تعالى بها ، والعالم منا بالشيء يستحيلُ أنْ يخبر الجزء مِنْ قلبِهِ الذي قام به العلم بخبرٍ كذب على غيرِ وَفْقِ علمِهِ ، غايتُهُ : أَنْ يجد في نفسِهِ تقدير الكذب ، لا الكذب وأيضاً : لوِ اتَّصفَ البارى تعالى بالكذب ولا تكونُ صفتُه إلا قديمة .. لاستحال اتصافه بالصدق مع صحة اتصافِهِ بهِ ; لأجلِ وجوبِ العلم له تعالى ، ففيه استحالة ما علمنا صحتَهُ
And is the indication of the miracle to the truthfulness of the messenger an intellectual indication, or a conventional one, or a habitual one according to circumstances? There are opinions. As for the first two, it is impossible for it to occur at the hand of a liar, because of the necessary consequence upon the first of contradiction of intellectual proof, and upon the second of breaking of His (mighty and majestic) promise; for verifying a liar is a lie, and lying upon Him (mighty and majestic) is impossible, because His speech is in accordance with His knowledge, so it is true, and if it were absent, knowledge its necessary condition would be absent, which is impossible, as you know from its necessity. If you say: we find a knowledgeable human sometimes informs about something with a lie. We say: our discussion is about the inner speech, not the verbal utterances, because it is impossible for the Exalted to be described by them. And a knowledgeable human is unable that the part of his heart in which knowledge resides should inform with a false report not in accordance with his knowledge; his utmost is that he finds in his soul the estimation of a lie, not the lie. Also: if the Exalted were described with lying, and His attribute would be eternal, then it would be impossible for Him to be described with truth despite the validity of His being described with it due to the necessity of His having knowledge, so therein is impossibility which we know its correctness.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage addresses the fundamental question of how a miracle indicates the truthfulness of a prophet. Sanusi presents three views: rational (aqliyya), conventional (wad'iyya), and habitual (adiyya) based on circumstances. For the first two views, it is impossible for a miracle to come from a liar. On the rational view, a liar performing a miracle would directly contradict rational proof that only a true prophet could do such a thing. On the conventional view, God has established a convention (wad') that miracles accompany truth; if a liar performed a miracle, God would be breaking His promise, which is impossible because God's speech (inner speech, khabar nafsi) always accords with His knowledge. The objection arises: humans sometimes lie about what they know. The answer distinguishes inner speech from verbal utterances. For God, only inner speech is relevant, and it is impossible for Him to lie internally. For humans, their inner speech also cannot contradict their knowledge; the lie is only verbal. A further argument: if God were to lie, His attribute of lying would be eternal, making it impossible for Him to also be truthful, but His knowledge necessitates truthfulness. Hence lying is impossible for God.

Key Terms
دلالة عقلية
دلالة وضعية
دلالة عادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that miracles indicate truth merely by habit or coincidence.
Correction: Sanusi shows that for rational and conventional views, the indication is stronger than habit; it is logically necessary.

Checkpoint
Why is a miracle impossible from a liar according to the conventional view?
A) Because a liar cannot perform any supernatural act
B) Because God's promise to only grant miracles to truthful prophets would be broken
C) Because miracles are rational proofs
D) Because the prophet must be morally good

Key Takeaway
Miracles indicate truth rationally and by God's promise; both make lying impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on a sign or experience to confirm a belief, only to later realize it could have been from a false source? How does Sanusi's argument about miracles challenge you to examine the foundations of your certainty about the truthfulness of the Prophet (PBUH)? Will you take time this week to learn more about al-dalala al-aqliyya to strengthen your conviction?
?
The passage says that God's speech is in accordance with His knowledge, so it is true. When you read the Qur'an, do you have a deep, rational conviction that every word is true because it comes from God's knowledge, or do you sometimes feel uncertainty? What step can you take today to move from habitual acceptance to intellectual certainty?
?
Sanusi argues that it is impossible for a miracle to come from a liar because it would break God's promise. Have you ever considered that God's truthfulness is the ultimate guarantee of the credibility of miracles? In a world full of false claims, how does this truth free you from doubt about the prophethood of Muhammad? Write down one specific ayah that proves God's truthfulness and commit to memorizing it this week.

Answering the Objection: Human Lying vs. God's Speech
Sanusi answers an objection about human lying and provides an additional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between inner speech and verbal utterances, and why human lying does not imply that God can lie.

Core Idea
Humans lie verbally, but inner speech never contradicts knowledge—and God's speech is only inner.

Sanusi anticipates an objection: if humans sometimes lie about what they know, why can't God? He answers by distinguishing inner speech (psychological reality) from verbal utterances. God is only described by inner speech, which always matches knowledge. Furthermore, if God could lie, His attribute would be eternal, making truth impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the objection that humans lie despite knowledge. Then read Sanusi's response carefully.
The objection is a natural question for a student of theology.
2
Understand the inner speech distinction
Sanusi says we are discussing inner speech (al-khabar an-nafsi), not verbal utterances. For God, only inner speech applies. For humans, the inner speech (the knowledge in the heart) cannot lie; only the verbal expression can be false.
This is a key theological concept.
3
Follow the additional argument
If God were to lie, His attribute of lying would be eternal, which would make it impossible for Him to also have the attribute of truth, but His knowledge necessitates truth. Thus lying is impossible.
This argument uses the principle that eternal attributes are mutually exclusive if contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل دَلالةُ المعجزة على صدق الرسولِ دَلالة عقلية أو وضعيّةٌ ، أو عاديَّةٌ بحسَبِ القرائنِ ؟ أقوال أما على الأوَّلين فيستحيلُ صدورها على يد الكاذب ; لما يلزم على الأَوَّلِ مِنْ نقضِ الدليل العقلي ، وعلى الثاني مِنَ الخُلْفِ في خبرِهِ جلَّ وعلا ; إذْ تصديق الكاذب كذب ، والكذب عليه جلَّ وعلا محالٌ ; لأنَّ خبرَهُ على وَفْق علمِهِ ، فيكون صدقاً ، فلو انتفى لانتفى العلم ملزومه ، وهو محال ; لما عرفتَ مِنْ وجوبِهِ فإن قلت : قد وجدنا العالِمَ منا بالشيء يخبرُ عنه بالكذب . قلنا : كلامنا في الخبر النفسي ، لا في الألفاظ ; لاستحالة اتصاف الباري تعالى بها ، والعالم منا بالشيء يستحيلُ أنْ يخبر الجزء مِنْ قلبِهِ الذي قام به العلم بخبرٍ كذب على غيرِ وَفْقِ علمِهِ ، غايتُهُ : أَنْ يجد في نفسِهِ تقدير الكذب ، لا الكذب وأيضاً : لوِ اتَّصفَ البارى تعالى بالكذب ولا تكونُ صفتُه إلا قديمة .. لاستحال اتصافه بالصدق مع صحة اتصافِهِ بهِ ; لأجلِ وجوبِ العلم له تعالى ، ففيه استحالة ما علمنا صحتَهُ
And is the indication of the miracle to the truthfulness of the messenger an intellectual indication, or a conventional one, or a habitual one according to circumstances? There are opinions. As for the first two, it is impossible for it to occur at the hand of a liar, because of the necessary consequence upon the first of contradiction of intellectual proof, and upon the second of breaking of His (mighty and majestic) promise; for verifying a liar is a lie, and lying upon Him (mighty and majestic) is impossible, because His speech is in accordance with His knowledge, so it is true, and if it were absent, knowledge its necessary condition would be absent, which is impossible, as you know from its necessity. If you say: we find a knowledgeable human sometimes informs about something with a lie. We say: our discussion is about the inner speech, not the verbal utterances, because it is impossible for the Exalted to be described by them. And a knowledgeable human is unable that the part of his heart in which knowledge resides should inform with a false report not in accordance with his knowledge; his utmost is that he finds in his soul the estimation of a lie, not the lie. Also: if the Exalted were described with lying, and His attribute would be eternal, then it would be impossible for Him to be described with truth despite the validity of His being described with it due to the necessity of His having knowledge, so therein is impossibility which we know its correctness.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage addresses the fundamental question of how a miracle indicates the truthfulness of a prophet. Sanusi presents three views: rational (aqliyya), conventional (wad'iyya), and habitual (adiyya) based on circumstances. For the first two views, it is impossible for a miracle to come from a liar. On the rational view, a liar performing a miracle would directly contradict rational proof that only a true prophet could do such a thing. On the conventional view, God has established a convention (wad') that miracles accompany truth; if a liar performed a miracle, God would be breaking His promise, which is impossible because God's speech (inner speech, khabar nafsi) always accords with His knowledge. The objection arises: humans sometimes lie about what they know. The answer distinguishes inner speech from verbal utterances. For God, only inner speech is relevant, and it is impossible for Him to lie internally. For humans, their inner speech also cannot contradict their knowledge; the lie is only verbal. A further argument: if God were to lie, His attribute of lying would be eternal, making it impossible for Him to also be truthful, but His knowledge necessitates truthfulness. Hence lying is impossible for God.

Key Terms
الخبر النفسي
الكذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since humans can lie about what they know, God could also lie about what He knows.
Correction: Human lies are only verbal; their inner speech always corresponds to their knowledge. God's speech is only inner, so it cannot contradict His knowledge.

Checkpoint
What kind of speech does Sanusi say is under discussion when talking about God's truthfulness?
A) Verbal utterances
B) Inner speech
C) Both inner and outer speech
D) Only human speech

Key Takeaway
God's speech is inner and always true; lying would make truth impossible eternally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's speech is always true and cannot contradict His knowledge. Do you live as if every promise of reward and punishment in the Quran is absolutely certain? Identify one promise you struggle to believe fully, and commit today to acting on it as if it were already true.
?
You are capable of lying, unlike God. Have you ever justified a 'small' lie thinking it would serve a good purpose? How does the purity of God's speech challenge your attitude toward truthfulness? Will you examine your conversations today for even subtle falsehoods?
?
The lesson shows that even a human's inner speech cannot contradict what they truly know. In what area of your life are you ignoring what your heart knows to be true, pretending otherwise? What one step can you take today to align your outer life with that inner truth?

The First Obligation: Knowledge of God
Sanusi defines the first obligation on the mukallaf and explains its prerequisite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first obligation upon a person who has reached puberty (mukallaf) is to use reason to attain certain knowledge of God through decisive proofs, and that this knowledge is a prerequisite for all other religious duties.

Core Idea
The first duty is to think—using proofs to know God.

Sanusi begins his theological treatise by establishing the most fundamental duty of every accountable person. Before any ritual act or legal obligation, there is an intellectual imperative: to seek certain knowledge of the Creator. This lesson unpacks that foundational obligation and explains why rational reflection takes priority over all else.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: praise, invocation, then the central statement about the first obligation.
The supplication for 'expansion of the chest' is a prayer for openness to knowledge.
2
Identify the first obligation
Find the sentence that states what is obligatory before everything else. Note the key terms: 'يُعمِلَ فكرَهُ' (employ his thought), 'العلم بمعبوده' (knowledge of his worshipped deity), and 'البراهين القاطعة' (decisive proofs).
3
Understand the condition and priority
The passage adds a condition: if knowledge was already obtained before puberty, then the person should move on to the next most important matters. This shows that the obligation is conditional on not already having certainty.
This reflects the principle of 'الأهم فالأهم' (the most important then the next).
4
Reflect on the supplication's significance
Sanusi explains why he prayed for 'sharh al-sadr' (expansion of the chest): it prepares the mind to receive knowledge and removes difficulty. Consider why this is an appropriate opening for a work on kalam.
The footnote clarifies the nuance between 'diq' (constriction) and 'haraj' (restrictiveness).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أول واجب على المكلف
الحمدُ للهِ ربِّ العالمينَ ، والصلاة والسلام على سيدنا ومولانا محمَّدٍ خاتم النبيِّينَ وإمام المرسلينَ ، ورضي الله أصحابِ رسولِ اللهِ أجمعينَ ، وعن التابعينَ ومَنْ تبعهم عن بإحسان إلى يوم الدين .
اعلم - شرحَ الله صدري وصدرَكَ ، ويَسَّرَ لنيل الكمال في الدارين أمري وأمرَكَ - : أنَّ أَوَّلَ ما يجبُ قبلَ كُلِّ شيءٍ على مَنْ بَلَغَ : أنْ يُعمِلَ فكرَهُ فيما يُوصِلُهُ إلى العلم بمعبودِهِ مِنَ البراهين القاطعة والأدلَّةِ الساطعة ، إلا أنْ يكونَ حصل له العلم بذلك قبل البلوغ ، فليشتغل بعده بالأهم فالأهم
الكلام فيما يتعلَّق بالحمدِ والصلاة على النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ شهير ، فلا نطيلُ بهِ ، ولا يخفى هنا حسنُ مناسبة الدعاء بشَرح الصدرِ الذي هو تهيئتُهُ لقَبولِ المعارفِ وفهمها ، وإزالة ضيقها عن حمل ذلكَ وحَرَجها (۱)
(۱) ولا يخفى أن ( حرجها ( أخص من ( ضيقها ( لأن الحرج هو الضيق الشديد ، فالمناسب أن لو طواه ؛ لأن إزالة الأعم تقتضي إزالة الأخص . « عكاري »
The first obligation upon the accountable person. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, and prayers and peace upon our master and patron Muhammad, the seal of the prophets and the imam of the messengers, and may Allah be pleased with all the companions of the Messenger of Allah, and with the followers and those who followed them with righteousness until the Day of Judgment. Know – may Allah expand my chest and your chest, and make easy for attaining perfection in both abodes my affair and your affair – that the first thing that is obligatory before everything upon one who has reached puberty is that he employ his thought in that which leads him to knowledge of his worshipped deity from decisive proofs and radiant evidences, unless he has obtained knowledge of that before puberty, then after it he should occupy himself with the most important then the most important. The discussion concerning praise and prayer upon the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is well-known, so we do not lengthen it. And it is not hidden here the good appropriateness of the supplication for expansion of the chest, which is its preparation for acceptance of knowledges and understanding them, and removal of its constriction and restrictiveness from bearing that. (1) (1) And it is not hidden that 'its restrictiveness' is more specific than 'its constriction', because restrictiveness is severe constriction, so the appropriate thing would be if he omitted it; because removal of the more general necessitates removal of the more specific. 'Ukkari.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is an opening exposition of the first obligation incumbent upon a person who has reached the age of accountability (mukallaf). The author begins with the basmala and praise, then states the rule: the very first obligatory act is to use one's intellect (fikr) to arrive at certain knowledge of God through clear, decisive proofs (barahin qati'a and adilla sati'a). This knowledge is a prerequisite for all other religious duties. However, if a person already possesses this knowledge from before puberty, he is excused from this initial reflection and should instead occupy himself with the most important things in order. After clarifying this central point, the author remarks that the discussion of praise and prayer on the Prophet is well-known, so he does not dwell on it. He then comments on the appropriateness of the supplication for 'expansion of the chest' (sharh al-sadr), which he interprets as a preparation of the mind to accept and understand knowledge, and to remove any difficulty in bearing it. A footnote clarifies that the word 'haraj' (restrictiveness) is more specific than 'diq' (constriction), and that by removing the more general, the more specific is automatically removed, so the author could have omitted the word.

Key Terms

أول واجب
براهين قاطعة
أدلة ساطعة
شرح الصدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the first religious duty is a physical act of worship like prayer or charity.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that the very first obligation is an intellectual act: to seek knowledge of God through proofs. This precedes all other duties.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the first thing obligatory upon someone who has reached puberty, assuming they do not yet have knowledge of God?
A) To perform the five daily prayers.
B) To seek knowledge of God through decisive proofs and radiant evidences.
C) To give charity.
D) To memorize the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The first duty of every accountable person is to use reason to attain certain knowledge of God through decisive proofs, and this must be done before all other obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly engaged your intellect to seek knowledge of God, or have you relied on inherited beliefs without personal conviction? What specific decisive proofs have you personally considered, and if none, what will stop you from starting today?
?
The text says the first obligation is to employ thought to gain knowledge of God. When you examine your daily priorities, does seeking this knowledge come first, or is it pushed aside by lesser concerns? What would change if you genuinely made it your first duty from this moment onward?
?
If you already possess knowledge of God, are you occupying yourself with 'the most important then the most important' as this lesson teaches? How do you determine what is most important in your life right now, and what one concrete step can you take today to realign your priorities with this first obligation?

The First Obligation is Shar'an, Not Aqlan
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila objection about silencing messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that the first obligation is known through revelation, not reason, and how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila objection using the 'shared premise' argument.

Core Idea
Revelation, not reason, defines what is obligatory — even the obligation to reflect.

Sanusi clarifies a key difference between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila: for us, all obligations come from revelation. The Mu'tazila object that if reflection were not rationally obligatory, messengers could be silenced. Sanusi responds by turning their own logic against them. This lesson walks through that debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Ash'ari baseline
Sanusi states that none of the duties, including rational reflection, are obligatory by reason alone; they are obligatory by revelation. This is the foundational claim.
2
Hear the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila argue that if reflection were not rationally obligatory, then people would never start reflecting, making messengers' efforts futile.
This is the 'silencing of messengers' argument.
3
Follow Sanusi's counter-argument
Sanusi says the objection is based on a 'shared premise' (مشترك) — the very problem the Mu'tazila raise also applies to their own view, so it is not binding.
This is a classic refutation by turning the opponent's argument against them.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the Ash'ari position is consistent: obligation depends on revelation, which comes through messengers. The objection is neutralized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يجب النظرُ ولا غيرُه عقلاً وقوله : ( أنَّ أوّلَ ما يجبُ ) أي : شرعاً ، وإِنَّما لم أُقيده بذلك كما وقع في الإرشادِ (۱) وغيره ؛ لعدم اختصاص القيد بهذا الواجب ، بل الأحكامُ كلُّها إنَّما تثبت عند أهل السنة بالشرع ، وحَكَمَتِ المعتزلة فيها العقل ، وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى الردُّ عليهم في محله (۲) ، إلا أنَّهم خَصُّوا هذا الموضع باعتراض ؛ وهو أن قالوا : لو لم يجب النظرُ عقلاً .. للزمَ إفحام الرسل ، وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ المكلَّفَ لا ينظرُ ما لم يعلم وجوبَه ، ولا يعلمُ وجوبَهُ ما لم ينظر . وأُجيبَ : بأنَّهُ مشترك ، والمشترك غير ملزم (۳) ؛ إذ لو وجبَ عقلاً لأفحم أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ وجوب النظر غير ضروري عندهم ؛ لتوقفه على مقدمات تفتقر إلى أنظار دقيقة
Rational reflection and anything else is not obligatory rationally. And his statement: 'Indeed the first of what is obligatory' means: by revelation. And I did not restrict it with that as occurred in al-Irshad and others because the restriction is not specific to this obligation; rather all rulings are only established for Ahl al-Sunna by revelation, while the Mu'tazila judge by reason in them. And the refutation of them will come in its place, God willing, except that they singled out this place with an objection, which is that they said: 'If rational reflection were not obligatory rationally, then it would lead to the silencing of the messengers.' And the explanation of the implication: that the responsible person does not engage in rational reflection as long as he does not know its obligation, and he does not know its obligation as long as he does not engage in rational reflection. And it was answered: that it is a shared premise, and a shared premise is not binding; for if it were obligatory rationally, it would also silence them, because the obligation of rational reflection is not self-evident for them, due to its dependence on premises that require precise reflections.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila objection. Sanusi first establishes that for Ahl al-Sunna, all obligations (including the first one, which is rational reflection) are known through revelation (شرع), not reason (عقل). The Mu'tazila object that if rational reflection were not rationally obligatory, then messengers would be silenced (إفحام الرسل) because people would never start reflecting. Sanusi responds with a 'shared premise' (مشترك) argument: the same objection applies to the Mu'tazila's own position, because their rational obligation of reflection also suffers from the need for prior knowledge. Thus the objection is not binding.

Key Terms
النظر
شرع
عقل
إفحام الرسل
المشترك غير ملزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Sanusi denies any rational obligation; he only affirms that obligation comes from revelation, not that reason has no role.
Correction: Reason is used to understand revelation, but obligation itself is known through revelation.

Checkpoint
What is the 'shared premise' argument used by Sanusi against the Mu'tazila?
A) The Mu'tazila's own position leads to the same problem of infinite regress.
B) Rational reflection is impossible without revelation.
C) The Mu'tazila have no proof for rational obligation.
D) Both sides agree that rational reflection is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection fails because the same problem applies to their own position; Sanusi's 'shared premise' refutation preserves the Ash'ari principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied more on your own reasoning and logic than on what Allah has revealed in the Qur’an or through His Messenger, especially when it comes to what is truly obligatory upon you? Recall a specific instance. What will you do differently from now on to ensure revelation takes precedence?
?
The Mu'tazila were concerned about 'silencing messengers' — but practically, in your own life, how have your intellect and personal desires silenced the message of the prophets? Identify one area where you have ignored a clear command from Allah because your intellect objected, and commit to a concrete act of obedience to reverse that today.
?
The shared premise argument reveals that both positions face the same difficulty. Where in your life do you condemn others for a flaw or difficulty while excusing the same in yourself? Name one situation, and write down a tangible step to apply the same standard to yourself that you apply to others.

Rational Reflection Does Not Require Prior Knowledge of Obligation
Sanusi argues that rational reflection does not depend on knowing its obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that rational reflection (nazar) does not depend on knowing that it is obligatory, either by natural custom or by sacred law.

Core Idea
Reflection on wonder is natural; obligation only requires the capacity to know.

Sanusi addresses a common objection: does one need to know that reflection is obligatory before reflecting? He answers with a double argument: habitually, God's custom ensures that people naturally wonder at marvels without needing a command; legally, obligation depends on the ability to know, not on actual knowledge. This establishes reflection as the first duty without circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two-part structure: 'Amma 'ādatan...' and 'Amma shar'an...'.
2
Understand the habitual argument
Sanusi says God's custom is that rational beings do not all turn away from reflecting on the wonders of creation, especially prophetic miracles. This shows reflection is naturally prompted without prior obligation knowledge.
This refutes the idea that one must know the obligation first.
3
Understand the legal argument
Legally, the obligation of reflection depends on the ability to know (tamakkun min al-'ilm), not on actually knowing the obligation. So one is not required to know it before reflecting.
This solves the regress problem.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that the first obligation is to employ one's thought (an yu'mila fikrahu). Reflect on how this argument defends the priority of nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحقُ : أنَّ النظر لا يتوقَّفُ على العلـم بـالـوجـوب لا عادةً ولا شرعاً : أَمَّا عادةً : فلأنَّ الله تعالى أجرى عادتَهُ وطردَ سُنتَهُ بعدم تواطؤ العقلاء على الإعراض عن النظر في عجائب الكائنات وغرائب المصنوعات، ومن أعظم ذلك ما تأتي بهِ الرُّسُلُ مِنْ خوارق العاداتِ . وأما شرعاً : فلأنَّ النظر وجوبُهُ مُتوقف على التمكَّنِ مِنَ العلم ، (1) لا على العلم) وقوله : ( أنْ يُعمِلَ فكرَهُ ( خبرُ ( أنَّ ) ، وحاصلُهُ : أَنَّ أَوَّلَ واجب النظر
And the truth is that rational reflection does not depend on knowledge of the obligation, neither habitually nor legally. As for habitually: because God Almighty has set His custom and established His sunnah in that the rational beings do not unanimously turn away from reflecting on the wonders of the universe and the marvels of created things, and among the greatest of that is what the messengers bring from the breaking of customs. And as for legally: because the obligation of rational reflection depends on the ability to know, not on actual knowledge. And his statement: 'that he should employ his thought' is the predicate of 'that', and its conclusion: that the first obligation is rational reflection.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage provides a second refutation of the Mu'tazila objection. Sanusi states the true position: rational reflection does not require prior knowledge of its obligation, either habitually or by sacred law. Habitually, God's customary practice ensures that people naturally wonder and reflect on astonishing phenomena, especially the miracles of prophets, without first knowing that they must reflect. Legally, the obligation of reflection depends on the capacity to know, not on actually knowing the obligation. Then he connects back to the statement from the text: the first obligation is to employ one's thought, i.e., to engage in rational reflection.

Key Terms
النظر
العادة
الشرع
التمكن من العلم
خوارق العادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an action cannot be obligatory without first knowing that it is obligatory.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that obligation requires only the capacity to know (tamakkun min al-'ilm), not actual knowledge of the obligation itself.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does rational reflection not depend habitually on knowing its obligation?
A) Because people are naturally driven to reflect on marvels, without prior obligation knowledge.
B) Because God commands reflection only after sending messengers.
C) Because all rational beings are created with innate knowledge of obligation.
D) Because reflection is only obligatory after adulthood.

Key Takeaway
Rational reflection is the first obligation, and it does not require prior knowledge of that obligation; it only requires the capacity to know.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the wonders of creation—the sky, the stars, the human body—do you find yourself naturally drawn to reflect on the One who made them, or have you become accustomed to ignoring that wonder? What would it take for you to reclaim that natural inclination today?
?
Sanusi says you don't need to know that reflection is obligatory before you are obligated to reflect. Right now, you possess the capacity to think and reason. Are you using that capacity to ponder the signs of God, or are you letting it waste on distractions? Will you set aside even five minutes today to deliberately reflect on one marvel of creation?
?
You claim to have the ability to know God through reflection. If that ability alone makes reflection your first duty, what is actually stopping you from fulfilling it? Is it laziness, fear of what you might find, or the noise of daily life? Answer honestly, and then name one concrete step you will take before the day ends to turn your mind toward your Creator.

Refutation of the Objection on Shubha's Varying Effects
Sanusi refutes an objection about shubha leading to ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that shubha inherently leads to ignorance, and why its apparent varying effects are due to external factors.

Core Idea
Shubha always leads to ignorance; variations come from the observer's state.

Sanusi addresses an objection that shubha cannot have a fixed consequence because it produces different results in different people. The mutakallimun argue that sometimes shubha leads to ignorance, sometimes to nothing, sometimes to doubt. Sanusi firmly replies that the inherent effect of shubha is always ignorance; the variation is due to the observer's prior knowledge or conflicting arguments. This passage clarifies the nature of shubha and defends its reliable harmful effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objection first, then Sanusi's response.
The objection is presented briefly; focus on Sanusi's rebuttal.
2
Identify Sanusi's core claim
Locate the sentence 'إن لازمها على الحقيقة الجهل'. This is the key: shubha's necessary consequence is ignorance.
3
Understand the two cases
Sanusi explains why a knower and one examining conflicting shubhas do not end up ignorant. For the knower, prior knowledge of the opposite blocks the shubha. For the conflicted, doubt arises from the conflict, not from a single shubha.
These cases do not change the nature of shubha itself.
4
Recognize the misconception
The objection assumes shubha's effect varies, so it has no fixed consequence. Sanusi corrects this: the consequence is fixed, but the result may be masked by other factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما احتج به المتكلمون؛ من اختلاف الشبهة حسب أن الناظر فيها ابتداءً تقوده إلى الجهل، والناظر فيها بعد العلم لا تقوده إلى شيء، والناظر فيها عقب نظره في شبهة على النقيض تقوده إلى الشك، وما اختلف لم يرتبط بشيء.. فغير مسلم؛ لأنّا نقول: إن لازمها على الحقيقة الجهل، وإنما انتفى عن العالم اعتقاد صدق نتيجتها في نفسها للعلم بضدها، لا العلم بالربط بينهما، وكذا الناظر فيها عقب النظر في شبهة، وليس شكه من مجرد الشبهة، بل من تعارض الشبهتين، وهو في الحقيقة تعاقب رأيين، لا استرابة بين معتقدين الذي هو الشلق.
And what the mutakallimun argued with: that the differing of the shubha (sophistical argument) according to the fact that the one who examines it initially is led by it to ignorance, and the one who examines it after knowledge is led by it to nothing, and the one who examines it after examining a contrary shubha is led by it to doubt; and what differs is not linked to anything... is not granted; because we say: its necessary consequence in reality is ignorance, and it is only removed from the knower's belief that its result is true in itself due to knowledge of its opposite, not knowledge of the link between them; and similarly the one who examines it after examining a shubha, his doubt is not from the mere shubha, but from the conflict of the two shubhas, and in reality it is a succession of two opinions, not a wavering between two beliefs which is al-shalaq [?].
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The mutakallimun object that shubha cannot have a fixed necessary consequence because its effect varies: sometimes it leads to ignorance, sometimes to nothing, sometimes to doubt. Sanusi replies that the necessary consequence of shubha is always ignorance in reality; the variation is due to the observer's state. The knower's prior knowledge of the opposite prevents the shubha from producing ignorance, not because the link is absent but because the belief in the opposite blocks it. Similarly, the doubt after examining two conflicting shubhas is not from a single shubha but from their conflict, which is a succession of opinions, not genuine skepticism.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha)
ناظر (nāẓir)
الجهل (al-jahl)
الشك (al-shakk)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shubha's effect varies, so it has no necessary consequence.
Correction: Sanusi insists its inherent consequence is always ignorance; external factors mask it.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the true necessary consequence of a shubha?
A) Doubt
B) Nothing
C) Ignorance
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
Shubha always leads to ignorance in reality, regardless of the observer's state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you encountered a shubha (specious argument) that seemed convincing at first? Did it lead you to ignorance about something true? How did prior knowledge of the truth protect you from being misled? Are you actively seeking that knowledge to guard against future shubhas?
?
Consider a time when you were torn between two conflicting arguments and felt doubt. According to Sanusi, is that doubt genuine skepticism or just a succession of opinions? How can you move from that state to certainty? What step will you take today to resolve a current doubt by seeking the truth?
?
Sanusi insists that shubha always leads to ignorance unless blocked by knowledge of the opposite. Are there areas in your life where you have allowed shubhas to persist unchecked, leading to ignorance about your faith or actions? What concrete step can you take this week to identify and reject a shubha that is causing you confusion?

Refuting the Claim that Shubha Would Be a Proof if Linked to Belief
Sanusi refutes the claim that shubha must be a proof if linked to a belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a shubha (ambiguous argument) is not a proof even if linked to a specific belief, and the key difference in terms of necessary premises.

Core Idea
Formal similarity does not make an ambiguous argument a proof.

Sanusi now addresses a second objection. Some argue that if a shubha were connected to a specific belief, it would actually function as a proof. But that cannot be, because a shubha is by definition something mistaken for a proof. This lesson unpacks Sanusi's rebuttal and clarifies the crucial distinction between a proof and a shubha.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening statement of the opponents: if shubha is linked to a belief, it would be a proof. Note the logical structure of their claim.
The opponents assume linkage implies proof.
2
Understand Sanusi's definition
Sanusi defines shubha as something ambiguous that the examiner mistakenly believes is a proof. This definition is key to his response.
3
Grasp the distinction by necessary premises
Sanusi explains that a proof has necessary premises or premises that reduce to necessary ones, while a shubha does not. This is the fundamental difference, even if they share formal structure.
Necessary premises are self-evident or derived from self-evident ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما احتجوا به أيضاً؛ من أن الشبهة لو كان لها ارتباط بعقد معين لكانت دليلاً، والتالي باطل؛ لأن حقيقة الشبهة: ما اشتبه أمرها على الناظر فاعتقدها دليلاً وليست بدليل.. فلا يلزم؛ لجواز اشتراك المختلفات في بعض اللوازم المشتبهة؛ فإن الدليل يفارق الشبهة وإن اشتركا في صورة النظم، فإن مقدمات الدليل ضرورية أو تنتهي إلى الضرورية، والشبهة ليست كذلك.
And what they also argued with: that if the shubha had a connection to a specific belief, it would be a proof; and the consequent is false, because the reality of shubha is: what is ambiguous to the examiner so he believes it is a proof while it is not a proof.. so it does not follow; because it is possible for different things to share some ambiguous concomitants; for the proof differs from the shubha even if they share the formal structure, for the premises of a proof are necessary or terminate in necessary premises, while the shubha is not so.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The opponents claim that if shubha were linked to a specific belief, it would actually be a proof, which is false because a shubha is by definition an ambiguous argument mistakenly taken as a proof. Sanusi responds that the linkage does not entail being a proof, because different things can share some ambiguous concomitants. A proof and a shubha may share formal structure, but a proof's premises are necessary or reduce to necessary ones, whereas a shubha's premises are not.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha)
دليل (dalīl)
لازم (lāzim)
ضروري (ḍarūrī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any argument that appears linked to a belief must be a proof.
Correction: Formal similarity is not enough; a proof requires necessary premises, while a shubha does not.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a proof (dalīl) from a shubha according to Sanusi?
A) A proof has more premises
B) A proof's premises are necessary or lead to necessary ones
C) A proof is always believed
D) A shubha has no formal structure

Key Takeaway
A proof differs from a shubha because its premises are necessary or reduce to necessary ones, while a shubha's premises are not, even if they share formal structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you have never critically examined. Could it be based on a shubha—an argument that looks like a proof but lacks necessary premises? What would it take to test its premises?
?
Have you ever defended a position by imitating the structure of a strong argument while ignoring weak premises? How can you develop the honesty to admit when your reasoning is a shubha?
?
This lesson says a proof requires necessary premises. In your daily life, do you demand necessity before accepting an important claim, or do you settle for mere appearances? What will you change starting today?

The Specific and General Opposites of Rational Consideration
Sanusi enumerates the specific and general opposites of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of opposites of rational consideration (nazar) into specific and general categories, and the theological implications of each.

Core Idea
Nazar has two kinds of opposites: those that involve the same object and those that prevent thought entirely.

Sanusi now turns to the opposites of nazar, explaining how certain mental states and physical conditions can impede rational consideration. He distinguishes between opposites that are specific to the object of thought and those that are general, affecting all thought. This classification helps clarify when nazar is impossible or redundant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own structure come through before interpreting.
The passage has a clear classification structure.
2
Identify the two categories
Sanusi divides opposites into specific (تخصه) and general (ت عمه وغيره). Find the definitions for each.
Specific opposites bring the object to mind; general opposites prevent it.
3
Analyze the specific opposites
Notice that knowledge and compound ignorance are listed as specific opposites. Why? Because considering something while already knowing it would be redundant. The text explains this as 'acquisition of the acquired'.
The example of a scholar testing another proof shows that this is not true nazar.
4
Understand the general opposites
Death, sleep, and forgetfulness are general opposites. They prevent the object from occurring to the mind at all, thus making nazar impossible.
Sanusi notes a debate: is the non-occurrence intellectual or customary?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أن للنظر في الشيء أضداداً تخصه، وأضداداً تعمه وغيره: فالخاصة: كل ما يوجب إخطار المنظور فيه بالبال؛ كالعلم به، والجهل به؛ أعني: المركب؛ لأنه لو نظر معهما لكان تحصيل الحاصل، قالوا: ونظر العالم في دليل آخر إنما هو لاختبار دلالته لا للاستدلال به، لأن وكالشك فيه والظن والوهم متى نظر في طرف لم يخطر بباله الطرف الآخر. وهل عدم الخطور للطرف الثاني الموجب للتنافي: عقلي أو عادي؟ فيه تردد للمتكلمين. والأضداد العامة: ما لا يخطر معها المنظور فيه بالبال؛ كالموت والنوم والنسيان وما في معناها. وبالجملة: فالنظر يضاد العلم وجملة أضداده.
And know: that for consideration (nazar) of a thing there are opposites specific to it, and opposites general to it and other things. The specific: everything that necessitates bringing the considered thing to mind; like knowledge of it, and ignorance of it; I mean: the compound [ignorance]; because if one considers with them it would be an acquisition of the acquired. They said: and the knower's consideration of another proof is only to test its indicatory power, not to use it as a proof, because and like doubt in it, and assumption, and illusion, when one considers one side the other side does not occur to his mind. And is the non-occurrence of the second side that necessitates opposition: intellectual or customary? There is hesitation among the mutakallimun. The general opposites: that with which the considered thing does not occur to the mind; like death, sleep, forgetfulness, and what is similar to them. And in summary: consideration (nazar) opposes knowledge and all its opposites.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the opposites of nazar (rational consideration). Sanusi divides them into specific opposites (those that directly involve bringing the object of consideration to mind, such as knowledge and compound ignorance) and general opposites (states like death, sleep, forgetfulness that prevent any mental presence). He explains that knowledge and ignorance are specific because they directly involve the object; considering while knowing leads to redundancy. He also notes a debated point: whether the non-occurrence of one side when considering another is logically necessary or merely customary. The passage ends with a summary: nazar contradicts knowledge and all its opposites.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
ضد (ḍidd)
العلم (al-ʿilm)
الجهل المركب (al-jahl al-murakkab)
الموت (al-mawt)
النوم (al-nawm)
النسيان (al-nisyān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think sleep or death are direct specific opposites of nazar, like knowledge and ignorance.
Correction: Sanusi distinguishes general opposites (death, sleep, forgetfulness) that prevent all thought from specific opposites that directly involve the object of consideration.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is considered a specific opposite of nazar (rational consideration)?
A) Death
B) Sleep
C) Knowledge
D) Forgetfulness

Key Takeaway
Nazar is opposed by specific mental states like knowledge and compound ignorance, and by general states like death, sleep, and forgetfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you considered something while already having knowledge of it? Did you realize it was futile? How can you avoid that in your pursuit of truth?

The Specific and General Opposites of Nazar
Sanusi explains the specific and general opposites of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of nazar's opposites into specific and general, and why only compound ignorance opposes nazar while simple ignorance does not.

Core Idea
Nazar's opposites: only compound ignorance blocks nazar; simple ignorance needs it.

Sanusi now turns to what opposes nazar. Not every state of mind prevents consideration; some, like simple ignorance, actually require it. In this passage, he distinguishes between opposites that are specific to nazar and those common to all mental acts, clarifying a key subtlety about ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the opening sentence carefully. Nazar has two kinds of opposites: specific (to nazar itself) and general (common with other acts). Notice how Sanusi sets up this division.
This is a typical kalam classification pattern.
2
Understand specific opposites
Focus on the examples: knowledge and compound ignorance. Why are these opposites? Because if you already have knowledge or a false belief (compound ignorance), seeking consideration is pointless—it's 'tahsil al-hasil' (acquiring the already acquired).
Simple ignorance is not listed as a specific opposite. Sanusi explicitly says it coexists with nazar.
3
Explore the examples and hesitation
Read the part about the scholar examining another evidence, and the states of doubt, conjecture, and illusion. Notice the hesitation: is the non-occurrence of the opposite side intellectual or customary? This shows kalam's reflective nuance.
4
Contrast with general opposites
The general opposites (death, sleep, forgetfulness) prevent even the occurrence of the object in mind. Contrast this with specific opposites where the object is brought to mind but in a way that renders nazar redundant.
The concluding sentence sums up: nazar contradicts knowledge and all its (general) opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ للنظر في الشيء أضداداً تخصه ، وأضداداً تعمُّهُ وغيره : فالخاصة : كلُّ ما يُوجِبُ إخطار المنظور فيه بالبال؛ كالعلم به، والجهل به ؛ أعني : المركَّبَ؛ لأنَّه لو نظرَ معَهما لكانَ تحصيل الحاصل. أما البسيط فلا ينافي النظر ، بل يجامعه ، وأتى بالعناية مع أنَّ الجهل إذا أُطلق انصرف للمركَّب ؛ نظراً للقول بأنه مشترك بين البسيط والمركب. قالوا : ونظرُ العالم في دليل آخرَ إِنَّما هو لاختبارِ دلالتِهِ لا للاستدلال به، وكالشكّ فيه والظن والوهم، متى نظر في طرف لم يخطر بباله الطرف الآخرُ. وهل عدم الخطورِ للطرف الثاني المُوجب للتنافي : عقلي أو عادي ؟ فيه تردُّد للمتكلمين . والأضداد العامةُ : ما لا يخطرُ معَها المنظور فيهِ بالبال؛ كالموتِ والنوم والنسيان وما في معناها. وبالجملة : فالنظرُ يُضادُّ العلم وجملة أضدادِهِ.
Know that for looking (nazar) into a thing there are opposites specific to it, and opposites common to it and to other things. The specific ones: everything that necessitates bringing the thing looked into to mind, such as knowledge of it, and ignorance of it—I mean compound ignorance; because if one looked with them it would be tahsil al-hasil (acquiring the already acquired). As for simple ignorance, it does not contradict nazar, but rather coexists with it. And he specified [compound ignorance] with care because when ignorance is unqualified it is understood as compound, on account of the view that it is shared between simple and compound. They said: The scholar's looking into another evidence is only to test its indication, not to use it for inference; and similarly doubt concerning it, and conjecture, and illusion, when one looks at one side the other side does not occur to his mind. And is the non-occurrence of the second side, which necessitates contradiction, intellectual or customary? There is hesitation among the theologians. The general opposites: that with which the thing looked into does not occur to mind, such as death, sleep, forgetfulness, and what is in their meaning. In summary, nazar contradicts knowledge and all its opposites.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the opposites of 'nazar' (intellectual consideration). It begins by stating that nazar has two kinds of opposites: specific and general. The specific opposites are those that involve bringing the object of consideration to mind, namely knowledge of it and compound ignorance of it. The author explains that if one already has knowledge or compound ignorance, engaging in nazar would be 'tahsil al-hasil' (acquiring the already acquired), which is impossible. He then clarifies that simple ignorance does not contradict nazar—they can coexist—and explains why he specifies compound ignorance: when the term 'ignorance' is used without qualification, it typically refers to compound ignorance according to some. The passage then considers examples: a scholar's examination of a second proof is for testing its indication, not for inference, so it is not true nazar; similarly, doubt, conjecture, and illusion prevent the opposite side from occurring to mind. A theological hesitation is raised: is the non-occurrence of the second side due to intellectual necessity or customary habit? This is undecided. The general opposites are those that prevent even the occurrence of the object of consideration, such as death, sleep, and forgetfulness. The passage concludes that nazar contradicts knowledge and all its opposites, meaning that nazar and knowledge cannot coexist in the same act.

Key Terms
النظر
الضد
العلم
الجهل المركب
الجهل البسيط
الشك
الظن
الوهم
الموت
النوم
النسيان
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All ignorance opposes nazar.
Correction: Only compound ignorance (false belief) opposes nazar; simple ignorance (lack of knowledge) does not and can coexist with nazar.

Checkpoint
Why does the author specify 'compound ignorance' as a specific opposite of nazar, and not simple ignorance?

B) Because compound ignorance involves a false belief, which prevents the need for nazar.
C) Because simple ignorance does not bring the object to mind, while compound ignorance does.
D) Because the author follows the Ash'ari school that only compound ignorance is considered ignorance.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is opposed by knowledge and compound ignorance specifically, and by death, sleep, and forgetfulness generally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
What general opposites—sleep, forgetfulness, the busyness of life—keep you from reflecting on the most important truths? What concrete step will you take tomorrow to carve out time for nazar?
