Does the Proof of Contingency Necessarily Indicate a Maker?
Sanusi presents the disagreement between al-Razi and al-Juwayni on the indication of contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute between al-Razi and al-Juwayni over whether the proof of the world's contingency directly necessitates a Maker or requires an additional premise.

Core Idea
Even after proving the world’s contingency, scholars disagree on whether the conclusion is immediate or needs further reasoning.

After establishing that the world is contingent (hadith), scholars debated the nature of the inference to God’s existence. Al-Razi held that the proof of contingency alone is sufficient for necessary knowledge of a Maker, while al-Juwayni and others thought an additional premise is needed. This lesson examines that dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the disagreement
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the part after 'And the imams have differed...'
2
Identify the two positions
Note that al-Razi says the indication is necessary (daruri), while al-Juwayni says it is theoretical (nazari) and needs something else.
The additional element is often the principle of possibility (imkan).
3
Understand the context
The passage says this disagreement occurs after establishing contingency, which is evident. So the dispute is not about the fact but about the inferential method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما نُقِلَ عن طائفةٍ مِنْ أهل العلم : أنَّ اللهَ معروفٌ بضرورةِ العقل ... إلى آخره : فإن أرادوا أنَّ النظر في معرفتِهِ تعالى ينتهي إلى الضرورة.. فمُسلَّمٌ ؛ لأنَّ معرفته جلَّ وعلا بل ومعرفة جميع عقائدِ الإيمانِ إنَّما هو بالبراهين ، والبراهينُ لا بدَّ أنْ تنتهي إلى مُقدِّماتٍ ضرورية ، وإلا لزمَ التسلسل ، ولم تنتج القطع الذي كُلِّفْنا بهِ في العقائدِ وإنْ أرادوا أنَّهُ معروفٌ بضرورة العقل بَدْءاً بحيثُ لا يفتقر إلى نظر أصلاً . . فلا خفاء في بطلان هذه المقالة وقد اختلف الأئمَّةُ بعد تحقيق المسألة حدوث العالم ببرهانه : هل دلالته بعد على وجودِ محدثِهِ ضرورية وإليه ذهب الفخرُ ، أم نظريةٌ يُحتاجُ معها إلى ضميمة شيءٍ آخر ، وإليه ذهب إمام الحرمين وجماعة مِنَ المُحقِّقِينَ ؟ على ما سيأتي تحقيقُهُ إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ تعالى (۱) ، فإِذا كانَ هذا الخلافُ بعد علمِ الحدوثِ للعالم المتضِحِ بحسَبِ الظاهرِ دلالتُهُ العقائد ؛ وهو علم وجودِهِ جلَّ وعلا الذي اتفقت عليه جميعُ العقلاء إلا مَنْ لا يُعتَدُّ بهِ ، وَلَئِن سَأَلْتَهُم مَّنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ في أظهرِ
And as for what is transmitted from a group of scholars that Allah is known by necessity of the intellect... etc.: If they mean that reflection on knowing Him Most High ends in necessity, then it is accepted; because knowledge of Him, Exalted and High, indeed knowledge of all tenets of faith, is only through proofs, and proofs must end in necessary premises, otherwise there would be infinite regress, and they would not produce the certainty which we are obligated to have in the doctrines. And if they mean that He is known by necessity of the intellect from the outset such that it does not require any reflection at all, then there is no hiddenness in the invalidity of this statement. The passage ends with a partial quote from the Quran: 'And if you ask them, Who created the heavens and the earth?' implying that even polytheists acknowledge this, showing that the point of disagreement is about the method of inference.

The Universal Agreement on the Existence of a Creator
Sanusi emphasizes universal agreement on God's existence despite scholarly disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see that the existence of God is acknowledged by almost all rational people, and that later disagreements are about the method of inference.

Core Idea
Even those who dispute the proof’s nature agree on God’s existence—the debate is about epistemology, not ontology.

Sanusi points out that the disagreement among scholars about the indication of contingency occurs despite universal agreement on God’s existence. He even quotes a Quranic verse showing that polytheists acknowledge a Creator. This highlights that the real issue is not whether God exists but how we come to know Him through reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage
Read from 'So when this disagreement occurs...' to the end.
2
Note the universality claim
Sanusi says all rational beings agree on God's existence except those not taken into account.
This includes polytheists who still admit a creator.
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
The verse 'Who created the heavens and the earth?' is cited to show that even idolaters answer 'Allah'.
This proves the point.

Refuting Necessary Knowledge Without Reasoning
Sanusi interprets and refutes a claim about necessary knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi accepts that reasoning ends in necessary premises but rejects that God is known without any reasoning, and how this refutes a common misunderstanding about necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge in faith comes at the end of reasoning, not the beginning.

This passage tackles a subtle point: some scholars claimed God is known by necessity of reason. Sanusi carefully distinguishes two meanings of this claim. One is true, the other false. This distinction is crucial for understanding the role of reasoning in Islamic theology and for avoiding a major error about how we come to know Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Sanusi is responding to a reported claim.
The passage uses an objection-and-answer format.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Sanusi presents two possible meanings of 'God is known by necessity of reason'. Find where he says 'if they mean...' and note the difference.
The first interpretation is accepted, the second is rejected.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
Sanusi explains why the first interpretation is valid: proofs must end in necessary premises to avoid infinite regress and produce certainty. Reflect on why this is essential for creedal knowledge.
4
Recognize the falsity of the second interpretation
Sanusi states plainly that if they mean knowledge of God is immediate without any reasoning, that is clearly false. Why? Because it contradicts the need for reflection that even the Qur'an commands.
This guards against a serious misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما نُقِلَ عن طائفةٍ مِنْ أهل العلم : أنَّ اللهَ معروفٌ بضرورةِ العقل ... إلى آخره : فإن أرادوا أنَّ النظر في معرفتِهِ تعالى ينتهي إلى الضرورة.. فمُسلَّمٌ ؛ لأنَّ معرفته جلَّ وعلا بل ومعرفة جميع عقائدِ الإيمانِ إنَّما هو بالبراهين ، والبراهينُ لا بدَّ أنْ تنتهي إلى مُقدِّماتٍ ضرورية ، وإلا لزمَ التسلسل ، ولم تنتج القطع الذي كُلِّفْنا بهِ في العقائدِ وإنْ أرادوا أنَّهُ معروفٌ بضرورة العقل بَدْءاً بحيثُ لا يفتقر إلى نظر أصلاً . . فلا خفاء في بطلان هذه المقالة
And as for what has been reported from a group of scholars: that God is known by necessity of reason... etc.: if they mean that reflection (al-nazar) concerning knowledge of Him, exalted, ends in necessity — then it is accepted; because knowledge of Him, may He be glorified and exalted, and indeed knowledge of all the tenets of faith (ʿaqāʾid al-īmān) is only through demonstrative proofs (al-barāhīn), and proofs must necessarily terminate in necessary premises (muqaddimāt ḍarūriyya), otherwise an infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would result, and the certainty (al-qaṭʿ) that we are obliged to have in matters of creed (al-ʿaqāʾid) would not be produced. And if they mean that He is known by necessity of reason from the outset such that it does not require any reflection at all — then there is no obscurity in the falsity of this statement.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer (سؤال وجواب) format. Sanusi reports a claim that God is known by necessity of reason. He then distinguishes two possible interpretations: (1) If they mean that the process of reflection (النظر) culminates in necessary premises, then this is accepted, because all demonstrative proofs must ultimately rest on self-evident premises to avoid infinite regress and to produce certainty in creedal matters. (2) If they mean that knowledge of God is immediate without any need for reflection, then this is clearly false. The passage emphasizes that all faith beliefs are attained through proofs, and proofs require necessary premises; but that does not mean the final belief itself is necessary without reasoning.

Key Terms
نظر
ضرورة
برهان
مقدمة ضرورية
تسلسل
قطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that 'necessary knowledge of God' means we know Him without any reasoning or reflection.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that necessary knowledge is the end point of reasoning, not the starting point. It means the premises of the proof are self-evident, not that the conclusion is reached without thought.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi accept regarding the claim that God is known by necessity?
A) He accepts it completely.
B) He accepts that reasoning ends in necessary premises.
C) He accepts it only for prophets.
D) He rejects it entirely.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi affirms that demonstrative proofs rest on necessary premises, but denies that knowledge of God is immediate without reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If you were to argue that Allah exists, would you be able to lay out a chain of reasoning from self-evident truths? Commit to writing down one proof for God's existence from the Qur'an or kalam by the end of this week.

The Extension of Necessity Across All Beliefs
Objection and answer on the scope of necessity in beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection against the necessity of all beliefs of faith and how the author responds by affirming the clarity of truth despite human rigidity.

Core Idea
Necessity in one belief does not automatically extend to all; truth remains clear.

This passage presents a key objection in classical kalam: even if we accept that some beliefs are rationally necessary, does that necessity apply to all required beliefs? The objector argues that disagreement shows lack of necessity. The author's answer reminds us that truth can be clear yet obscured by intellectual stubbornness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector cites a clear belief (that Allah created all things) and questions whether necessity extends to more obscure beliefs.
Notice the use of 'ضرورة' (necessity) as a technical term.
2
Identify the core argument
The objector points to disagreement and error among rational people as evidence that necessity does not cover all beliefs.
3
Understand the author's response
The author asserts that the truth is clear enough but sometimes needs explanation due to rigidity.
The author uses a prayer to emphasize humility.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this objection challenges the scope of rational necessity in theology.
This connects to broader discussions of certainty and doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَيَقُولُنَّ خَلَقَهُنَّ الْعَزِيزُ الْعَلِيمُ ) [الزخرف : ٩] . . فكيف بأغمض منه ؟! ولَئِنْ سُلِّمَتِ الضرورةُ في هذهِ العقيدة الواضحة تسليماً جدليّاً وأنَّ كلَّ مُظهر للإيمان لا يُقلّد فيها .. فمِنْ أينَ تلزم الضرورة في سائر العقائدِ المشترطَةِ في الإيمانِ ، وقد عُلِمَ تشتُتُ أنظارِ العقلاء فيها ، ووقوع الغلط فيها لأكثرهم ، ولم يُوفّق لإصابة الحقِّ فيها إلا الأقل ؟! والحق في المسألة كانَ أوضحَ مِنْ أن تفتقر معَهُ إلى مثل هذا الطول ، لكن قد يُضطَرُّ إلى بيانِ الواضح بسبب خفائه لجمودِ [القرائح؟] نسأله سبحانَهُ أنْ يُريَنا الحق حقاً ويُوفِّقَنا لاتباعِهِ ، وأَنْ يُريَنا الباطل باطلاً ويعيننا على اجتنابه
Surely they say, 'The Mighty, the Knowing created them' [Az-Zukhruf:9]... So how about something more obscure than that?! And if necessity is granted in this clear belief argumentatively, and that every one who manifests faith does not imitate in it, then from where does necessity apply to the other beliefs required in faith, when the diversity of opinions of the intellects among them is known, and error in them occurs for most of them, and only the few are guided to the truth?! And the truth in the matter is too clear to need such length with it, but one may be compelled to explain the obvious due to its being hidden because of rigidity of [؟] -- we ask Him, exalted, to show us truth as truth and guide us to follow it, and to show us falsehood as falsehood and help us avoid it.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the necessity of belief in all doctrines of faith. It begins by citing a Quranic verse (Az-Zukhruf 43:9) that asserts that all things are created by Allah, the Mighty, the Knowing. The objector then asks: if this clear belief is so obvious, how about even more obscure matters? The objection argues that even if we grant, for the sake of argument, that there is a necessity (ضرورة) in this clear belief and that every person who professes faith does not merely imitate in it—yet from where does this necessity extend to all the other beliefs required for faith? The objector points out that intellectual opinions differ on these matters, most people err, and only a few are guided to the truth. The author's answer is that the truth in this matter is too clear to need such lengthy argument, but sometimes it is necessary to explain something obvious because people's minds are rigid. The passage ends with a prayer: may Allah show us truth as truth and guide us to follow it, and show us falsehood as falsehood and help us avoid it.

Key Terms
ضرورة
عقيدة
باطل
حق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies that any belief is rationally necessary.
Correction: The objector accepts necessity in one clear belief but questions its extension to all beliefs of faith.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the objector's main point about necessity in beliefs?
A) Necessity applies only to clear beliefs.
B) Necessity does not automatically extend to all beliefs.
C) Everyone must use reason for every belief.
D) Only the belief in God's existence is necessary.

Key Takeaway
Rational necessity in one clear belief does not prove necessity in all beliefs; disagreement does not negate truth, but truth can be hidden by intellectual rigidity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doctrine that many intelligent people reject, do you quietly conclude that truth is uncertain, or do you search your own heart for rigidity that might be hiding clarity?
?
The author prays to be shown truth as truth and falsehood as falsehood. When did you last pray that prayer with full sincerity, or do you assume you've already arrived?
?
If most people err on key matters of faith, what makes you so sure you are among the few guided to the truth? Does your certainty rest on rational necessity or on comfortable imitation?

The Four Types of Inference and Knowledge of Allah
Sanusi classifies inference into four types and identifies those valid for knowing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's classification of four types of inference and which ones are valid for knowing Allah.

Core Idea
Only inference from effect to cause and from co-necessitants can lead to knowledge of Allah.

Before we can reason about Allah, we must understand the types of logical inference and which are applicable. Sanusi lays out a clear classification of four types of inference, then evaluates each type for its suitability in knowing the Necessary Being. This passage forms the methodological foundation for all subsequent arguments in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the four types of inference carefully, noting how each is defined with examples.
Pay attention to the examples: fire, boiling water, etc.
2
Identify the ruling
After the classification, Sanusi states which types are valid for knowing Allah. Find that statement and note the reasoning.
He explicitly says the second and fourth are suitable.
3
Understand the reasoning
Why are the first and third types invalid? Because Allah has no cause. Reflect on this crucial point.
This is due to the necessity of Allah's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية
في أنواع الاستدلال
اعلم أن الاستدلال على أربعة أضرب:
الأول: الاستدلال بالسبب على المسبب، كالاستدلال بمس النار مثلاً على احتراق الممسوس.
الثاني: عكسه، وهو الاستدلال بالمسبب على السبب، كالاستدلال باحتراق الشيء مثلاً على مس النار له، ومنه الاستدلال بوجود الأثر على وجود المؤثر.
الثالث: الاستدلال بأحد مسببي سبب واحد على المسبب الآخر، كالاستدلال بغليان الماء المركب في آنية على النار مثلاً على حرارته، فإن غليانه وحرارته مسببان عن سبب واحد، وهو مجاورة النار.
الرابع: الاستدلال بأحد المتلازمين على الآخر، كالاستدلال بوجوب كونه جل وعلا عالماً على وجوب قيام العلم به، ومنهم من رد هذا إلى القسم الثاني، وهو الاستدلال بالمسبب على السبب.
فإذا عرفت هذا: فالذي يصلح من هذه الأنواع لمعرفته تعالى - النوع الثاني والرابع. أما الأول - وهو الاستدلال بالسبب على المسبب - فمحال في حقه تعالى؛ لوجوب وجوده، فيستحيل أن يكون له سبب. وبهذا يبطل في حقه القسم الثالث. (1)
(1) لكون المسبب يستلزم السبب. (عكاري، ق ٦٦)
The Second Introduction: On the Types of Inference. Know that inference is of four types: First: inference from the cause to the effect, like inferring from touching fire, for example, to the burning of the touched thing. Second: its opposite, which is inference from the effect to the cause, like inferring from the burning of a thing, for example, to the touching of fire to it, and from it [is] inference from the existence of the effect to the existence of the cause. Third: inference from one of two effects of one cause to the other effect, like inferring from the boiling of water placed in a vessel on fire, for example, to its heat; for its boiling and its heat are two effects from one cause, which is the proximity of fire. Fourth: inference from one of two co-necessitants to the other, like inferring from the necessity of His – Most High and Exalted – being knowing to the necessity of knowledge subsisting in Him. And some of them returned this to the second division, which is inference from the effect to the cause. So if you have understood this: that which is suitable from these types for knowledge of Him – Most High – [are] the second and fourth types. As for the first – which is inference from the cause to the effect – it is impossible with respect to Him – Most High – due to the necessity of His existence, so it is impossible that He have a cause. And by this same [reasoning] the third division is invalidated with respect to Him. (1) (1) Because the effect necessitates the cause. (Ukkari, Q 66)
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a classification of types of inference (al-istidlal) followed by a ruling on which are valid for knowing Allah. The author begins by stating that inference is of four types. The first is inference from cause to effect, e.g., inferring burning from touching fire. The second is the opposite, inference from effect to cause, e.g., inferring the touch of fire from the burning. The third is inference from one effect of a single cause to another effect of the same cause, as in boiling and heat both resulting from fire. The fourth is inference from one co-necessitant to another, such as inferring God's knowledge from the necessity of knowledge subsisting in Him. The author then notes that some scholars reduce the fourth to the second type (since co-necessity can be seen as effect-to-cause). After this classification, he applies the types to the knowledge of Allah: only the second and fourth types are suitable. The first type (cause to effect) is impossible for Allah because He has no cause, being necessarily existent. Likewise, the third type is invalidated because it would require a cause for Him, which is impossible.

Key Terms
استدلال
سبب
مسبب
متلازمان
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that inference from cause to effect is valid for knowing Allah.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies it is impossible because Allah has no cause, being necessarily existent.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which types of inference are valid for knowing Allah?
A) The first and second
B) The second and fourth
C) The first and third
D) The third and fourth

Key Takeaway
Sanusi establishes that only effect-to-cause and co-necessitant inferences are valid for knowing Allah, because Allah has no cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you ever unconsciously imagine that He is caused or limited by something? How would the understanding that He has no cause change the way you trust in His power and independence?
?
Reflect on your daily reasoning: do you more often look at creation to infer the Creator (effect to cause), or do you sometimes try to reason backwards from Allah's actions to something else? Which of the four types do you tend to use, and does it align with what Sanusi says is valid?
?
Sanusi says that inference from co-necessitants is valid, meaning that from one attribute of Allah you can deduce another. Choose one attribute you believe in (e.g., His mercy) and trace a necessary implication that follows from it. Does this change how you relate to that attribute in your worship or trust?

The Major Premise is Theoretical but Near
Sanusi presents two cases regarding the possible and states that the major premise is theoretical but near.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the major premise (that every possible needs a cause) is considered theoretical yet close to intuition, and how two different views on the possible lead to the same conclusion.

Core Idea
The major premise is not intuitive but theoretical—though very close to it.

In this passage, Sanusi clarifies the epistemological status of a key premise in the proof for God's existence. He first presents two possible views about the relationship between existence and non-existence for a possible entity. Then he corrects a common misunderstanding: the knowledge of this premise is not intuitive but is obtained through near reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two views
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi presents the two views on the possible: existence and non-existence are equal, or non-existence is more appropriate. Note which view he chooses.
The chosen view is that existence and non-existence are equal for the possible.
2
See the implication of the second view
Notice that the second view makes the need for a Creator even more evident, otherwise preponderance without a preponderator would occur.
3
Learn the status of the major premise
Read the correct position: knowledge of the major premise is theoretical (nazari) but obtained through near reasoning (nazar qarib).
This corrects the mistaken belief that it is intuitive (daruri).
4
Recognize the common mistake
Understand that some people mistakenly thought the knowledge is intuitive because the reasoning is so close.
The nearness of the reasoning led to the confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إن قلنا : إِنَّ الوجود والعدم بالنسبة إلى الممكن مستويانِ وهو المختار ، أمَّا إِنْ قلنا : إنَّ العدمَ أَولى بهِ مِنَ الوجودِ ؛ لَقَبولِهِ إِيَّاهُ بلا سبب .. فأظهرُ في الاحتياج إلى الصانع ; لئلا يلزم ترجيح الوجودِ المرجوح بلا مُرجح . والصحيح (١) : أنَّ العلم بتلك المقدّمة الكبرى نظري ، إلا أنَّهُ يحصل بنظرِ قريبٍ كما قرَّرناه الآنَ ، ولأجلِ قربِهِ ظَنَّ قومٌ أَنَّ ذلكَ العلم ضروري
This if we say: existence and non-existence with respect to the possible are equal, and that is the chosen view. But if we say: non-existence is more appropriate for it than existence, because it accepts it without cause, then it is clearer in need for the Maker, so that there is no implication of preponderance of the lower existence without a preponderator. And the correct view (1) is that knowledge of that major premise is theoretical, but it is obtained by near reasoning as we have now established, and because of its nearness some people thought that that knowledge is intuitive.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a two-part argument about the status of the major premise in the proof for the Creator. First, Sanusi presents two possible views about the relationship between existence and non-existence for a possible entity. The first view, which he says is the chosen one, holds that existence and non-existence are equally possible. The second view holds that non-existence is more appropriate for the possible because it can accept it without a cause—this second view makes the need for a Creator even more evident, since otherwise the preponderance of existence over non-existence would be without a preponderator. Then, Sanusi states the correct position: the knowledge of the major premise (that every possible needs a cause) is theoretical (nazari) and not intuitive (daruri). However, it is obtained through close reasoning (nazar qarib). This closeness led some people to mistakenly believe it was intuitive. This explanation follows the logic of the text: first presenting the two cases, then correcting a misunderstanding.

المقدمة الكبرى
نظري

الترجيح بلا مرجح

Correction: Sanusi clarifies that it is theoretical (nazari), but the reasoning is so close that it appears intuitive.

Refuting the Innateness of Knowledge in Children
Sanusi refutes al-Razi's innateness claim about the major premise in children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the claim that knowledge of the major premise is innate in children by distinguishing between necessary and theoretical knowledge.

Core Idea
Children are not born with all knowledge; they actively reason from an early age.

Sanusi now addresses a powerful objection raised by Fakhr al-Razi that the necessity judgment is self-evident even in children. If the premise were truly innate, wouldn't children affirm it? Sanusi carefully dismantles this, showing that children do engage in theoretical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage and note al-Razi's exaggeration that the premise is in the innate nature of all children.
The objection is that children would universally know the premise.
2
See the concession
Sanusi allows that some discerning children may have a natural disposition, but only for most of them, not all.
3
The key rebuttal
Even if conceded, children are not limited to necessary knowledge; they acquire many theoretical sciences.
Sanusi rejects the implication that children have only necessary knowledge.
4
The evidence from observation
Sanusi points to observable reality—children are not free from theoretical reasoning, especially near ones free from doubts.
This shows active reasoning in children.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا مبالغةُ الفخر بأنَّهُ في فطرةِ الصبيان : فممنوع عمومه في جميعهم ، وإن أراد في فطرةِ أكثر مميّزيهم .. فمُسلَّم ، لكن لا تُسلّم أنَّهُ لا علم لمميَّزيهم إلا الضروري حتى يلزمَ ما ذَكَرَ ، كيف ونحن نرى الصبيان لا ينفكُونَ عن علوم نظريَّةِ لا سيما القريبة التي لا تعارضها شبهة ويتمخض العقلُ فيها ؟!
And as for the exaggeration of al‑Razi that it is in the innate nature of children, its generality over all of them is denied. And if he means the nature of most of their discerning ones, then it is accepted, but it is not accepted that there is no knowledge for their discerning ones except the necessary, so that what he mentioned follows. How so? For we see children not free from theoretical sciences, especially the near ones that no doubt opposes and in which the mind is fully engaged.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection by Fakhr al‑Razi who claimed that the major premise is innately present in children. Sanusi responds by first denying the claim's generality over all children. If al‑Razi meant only most discerning children, that is conceded. But even then, it does not follow that children have only necessary knowledge; they possess many theoretical sciences, especially those that are 'near' (qariba) and free from countervailing doubts. This shows that the premise is not purely intuitive for children, as children's minds actively engage in theoretical reasoning.

Refutation of Innateness in Animals
Sanusi refutes the claim that the major premise is innate in animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi rejects the claim that animals possess innate universal propositions and how he distinguishes between sensory association and rational distinction.

Core Idea
Animal fear is from imagination, not rational understanding.

Sanusi now tackles a particularly bold claim: that even animals have the major premise ingrained in their nature. Using al-Razi's own example of a donkey and a wooden beam, he shows that what looks like rational inference is actually mere sensory association. This lesson walks through his refutation and the key distinction between imagination and true knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim is stated, then a counterexample is given, then an explanation.
2
Understand the claim being refuted
Identify the claim that the major premise is ingrained even in animals. Compare this with the previous lesson where the claim was about humans.
3
Analyze the donkey example
Notice the hypothetical: a donkey never beaten with a beam would not fear its sound. Then see how repeated pain leads to association, not rational knowledge.
The key is that the donkey does not form a universal proposition.
4
Distinguish imagination from rational distinction
Sanusi compares the donkey to a human recoiling from a striped rope. Both are based on sensory association, not rational understanding. This is the core refutation.
The term 'tamyiz 'ilmi' is the opposite of 'takhayyul'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المبالغةُ بأنَّهُ مركوز أيضاً في فطرة البهائم بدليل ما ذَكَرَهُ في صوتِ الخشبةِ : فمِنْ أعجبِ ما يذكرُ أنَّ البهائمَ تُدرِكُ قضايا كليَّةً ولوازمها !! فلو قُدِّرَ حمارٌ أو حيوان غيرُهُ لم يُضرَبْ قطَّ بخشبة .. لم يَنفِرْ مِنْ صوتها ألبتة ، ولكن إذا تكرّرَ عليهِ ذلكَ التألُمُ عند سماعِها تخيَّلَ مِنْ حِسِّها الألم ; لمقارنتِهِ المُؤلِمَ ، وعدم التمييز والانفكاك في خيالِهِ ، كما أنَّ السليمَ يَنفِرُ مِنَ الحبل المبرقش لمقارنته الأذى عندَه لهذا الشكل ، وهذا مِنَ الخيالاتِ ، لا مِنَ التمييز العلمي ، والله أعلمُ
And as for the exaggeration that it is also ingrained in the nature of animals, based on his example about the sound of the wooden beam, it is among the most astonishing things mentioned: that animals grasp universal propositions and their implications. If one supposes a donkey or other animal that has never been beaten with a beam, it would not recoil from its sound at all. But if that pain upon hearing it recurs to it, it imagines the pain from its sensation due to the accompaniment of the painful thing, and the lack of distinction and separation in its imagination. Just as a healthy person recoils from a striped rope due to the accompaniment of harm in his experience with that shape. This is from imaginations, not from rational distinction. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage refutes al‑Razi's claim that the major premise is ingrained in animals, using his own example of the sound of a wooden beam. Sanusi finds it astonishing to claim that animals grasp universal propositions. He argues that an animal that has never been beaten with a beam would not fear its sound. Only after repeated painful associations does the animal imagine pain upon hearing the sound, due to sensory association, not through rational distinction. Similarly, a person recoils from a striped rope because of past harm. This reaction is based on imagination (takhayyul) and sensory association, not on rational conceptual knowledge (tamyiz 'ilmi). Thus, animals do not possess the universal premise innately.

Key Terms
بهائم
قضايا كلية
تخيل
تمييز علمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals fear because they rationally understand the sound is dangerous.
Correction: Animals only learn through sensory association; they do not form universal propositions.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does a donkey that has never been beaten with a beam not fear its sound?
A) The donkey knows the sound is harmless through innate knowledge
B) The donkey has not yet developed rational understanding
C) The donkey has no prior association of the sound with pain
D) The donkey is naturally fearless

Key Takeaway
Animals do not grasp universal propositions; their reactions are based on sensory association, not rational understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What beliefs do you hold that might be, like the donkey's fear, merely associations from repeated experiences rather than conclusions you have rationally examined? Will you test one this week against the standard of certainty (yaqin)?
?
Sanusi shows that imagination (takhayyul) is not true knowledge. In your own worship or understanding of Allah, where have you been led by emotion or habit rather than by rational reflection on revelation and creation? How can you begin to distinguish?
?
The donkey cannot separate the sound of the beam from the pain it experienced. Are there people, events, or ideas you have automatically associated with harm without rationally analyzing the link? What would it cost you to honestly question that association?

The Argument from Pure Possibility and Its Distinction
Sanusi explains the distinction of the pure possibility argument and its logical structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unique distinction of the argument from pure possibility and how it proves the existence of a necessary being.

Core Idea
The argument from pure possibility proves a necessary cause without assuming the world's creation.

Sanusi now clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction between the argument from pure possibility and other proofs for God's existence. In the pure possibility argument, knowledge of the world's createdness follows from knowledge of the Maker, not the reverse. He then unpacks the logical steps that lead to establishing a being that is necessary in itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the four methods
Sanusi mentions that there are four methods, but focuses on the argument from pure possibility, which is distinct from the others in how it orders knowledge.
The other methods are not discussed in detail here.
2
Grasp the key distinction
Notice that in this argument, knowledge of the world's creation comes after knowledge of the Maker, whereas in other arguments it precedes. This is the defining difference.
3
Walk through the logical deduction
Follow the steps: the world is possible, so it needs a cause; that cause must be necessary to avoid circularity or infinite regress.
Each step follows necessarily from the previous.
4
Recognize the refutation of circularity and regress
Sanusi explicitly states that circularity (دور) and infinite regress (تسلسل) are impossible, which forces the conclusion of a necessary being.
This is a standard argument in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أربعة (١)، لأنَّه أسقط منها الطريقين الأخيرين ؛ لتركبهما مِنَ الأولين .
والفرق بين الاستدلال بطريق الإمكانِ المُجرَّدِ وبينَ غيرِهِ مِنَ الطرقِ (۲) : أنَّ العلم بحدوثِ العالم يتأخَّرُ في طريقِ الإمكان المجرَّدِ عنِ العلم بالصانع ، وفي غيرِهِ يتقدَّمُ .
وبيانه : أنا إذا حقَّقْنا أنَّ العالَمَ ممكن بذاتِهِ ، ويدلُّ على ذلكَ افتقاره ، وأنَّ كلَّ ممكن بذاتِهِ مِنْ حيثُ هو هو قابل للوجود والعدم ، فالوجودُ لهُ ليسَ مِنْ ذاتِهِ ، وكلُّ ما ليسَ لهُ الوجودُ مِنْ ذاتِهِ فالوجود لهُ مِنْ غيرِهِ ، ثم ذلك الغيرُ لا بدَّ وأنْ يكونَ واجب الوجودِ لذاتِهِ ، وإلا افتقر إلى ما افتقر إليه العالم ، ودارَ أو تسلسل على ما يأتي إنْ شاءَ الله بيانه في دليل قدم الصانع (۳) ، والدور والتسلسل محالانِ ، فثبت العلم بوجود مؤثر واجب لذاته
Four (1), because he omitted the last two methods since they are composed of the first two.
And the difference between reasoning by way of pure possibility and other ways (2): that knowledge of the world's creation is delayed in the way of pure possibility after knowledge of the Maker, while in others it precedes.
Its explanation: When we verify that the world is possible in itself, and its dependence indicates that, and that every possible in itself as such is receptive to existence and non-existence, so existence for it is not from its essence, and everything that does not have existence from its essence, then existence for it is from something other than it. Then that other must necessarily be necessary of existence in itself, otherwise it would depend on what the world depends on, leading to circularity or infinite regress, as will be explained in the proof of the eternity of the Maker (3), God willing. And circularity and infinite regress are impossible. Thus knowledge of the existence of an effective agent necessary in itself is established.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two methods of proving the existence of God: the argument from pure possibility and other arguments. The key difference: in the pure possibility argument, knowledge of the world's creation comes after knowledge of the Maker, whereas in other arguments it precedes. The argument proceeds: we first verify that the world is possible in itself (indicated by its dependence). Every possible being, considered in itself, is equally receptive to existence or non-existence, so its existence is not from itself. Therefore it must have a cause outside itself. That cause must be necessary in itself, otherwise it would itself be possible and require another cause, leading to circularity or infinite regress, both impossible. Thus we establish the existence of a necessary being. However, this only proves a necessary cause, not necessarily a voluntary creator, which leads to the next section.

Key Terms
إمكان مجرد
واجب الوجود
دور
تسلسل
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from pure possibility already proves that the world is created (temporal).
Correction: The argument only proves a necessary cause; it does not prove that the world is temporal. The temporality of the world is established through other arguments.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between the argument from pure possibility and other arguments?
A) In pure possibility, knowledge of the world's creation comes after knowledge of the Maker.
B) In pure possibility, knowledge of the world's creation comes before knowledge of the Maker.
C) They are the same.
D) Pure possibility does not prove a necessary being.

Key Takeaway
The argument from pure possibility establishes the existence of a necessary being solely from the nature of possibility, without assuming the world's temporal creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just followed a logical proof that everything possible depends on a necessary being. But do you actually live as if your existence is solely from God? In what areas of your life do you still act as if you are independent? Will you today recognize your absolute dependence in your heart and actions?
?
The argument shows that without a necessary being, there would be an infinite regress of dependencies. Have you ever reflected on the fact that your own existence is contingent and could be nothing without God's constant sustaining? How does this realization affect your daily attitude towards God—does it inspire humility and gratitude, or do you brush it aside?
?
Sanusi says that in this argument, knowledge of the world's creation comes after knowledge of the Maker. That means you don't need to first prove the world is created to know God. But in your own journey of faith, do you rely more on seeing signs of creation or on this logical reasoning? Which is more firm in your heart? Will you strive to strengthen your certainty through both?

The Proof's Structure and the Impossibility of Self-Creation
Sanusi distinguishes this proof and argues against self-creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's proof distinguishes itself from other proofs, why the major premise is omitted, and the logical argument against self-creation.

Core Idea
You cannot create yourself because you needed a cause to exist in the first place.

Sanusi now clarifies a key methodological point: in this particular proof, knowledge of the world's temporality comes only after knowing the Creator's existence. He then unpacks the proof's internal logic, showing why the major premise can be taken for granted and why self-creation is impossible. This lesson walks you through each step of his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once without pausing, then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in before analyzing.
Notice the compact style: Sanusi assumes you know the major premise already.
2
Understand the distinction between proofs
The first sentence says that in this method, knowledge of the world's temporality is delayed until after knowing the Creator. Other proofs might work differently. Reflect on why this ordering matters.
This distinguishes the proof from those that start from the world's temporality.
3
Identify the minor premise and the omitted major premise
The proof uses 'I was not, then I was' as the minor premise. The major premise 'Everyone who was not then was has a Creator' is omitted because it is self-evident. Sanusi says it was left out 'due to knowledge of it'.
This is a common teaching technique: emphasize what is obvious rather than repeating it.
4
Follow the argument against self-creation
Sanusi argues that if you could create yourself, you could create another being. But that is impossible, so self-creation is impossible. The key link is that ability to create one possible thing implies ability to create any possible thing, because all possibles are equal in possibility.
The argument relies on the parity of possible things regarding the attachment of power.
5
Understand the final clarification about 'تحصيل الحاصل'
The last sentence explains that choice of existence cannot be an effect in something already existing, because that would be 'realizing what is already obtained'—a redundant action. This reinforces that creation must be from non-existence.
This ties back to the impossibility of self-creation: you can't cause yourself because you already exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأنت ترى كيف تأخَّرَ العلم بحدوثِ العالم في هذه الطريقة عنِ العلم بوجودِ الصانع ، فقد ظهر الفرقُ بينَ هذه الطريقةِ وغيرِها مِنَ الطرق . قوله : ( فتعلم أنَّ لكَ مُوجِداً أوجدَكَ ) يعني : غيرَكَ ؛ بدليل ما بعد ، وهذا نتيجة الدليل المذكور ، إلا أنَّه استغنى فيهِ بذكرِ المقدمة الصغرى ؛ وهي قولنا : ( أنا لم أكن ثم كنتُ ) ، وحذفَ الكبرى ؛ وهي قولنا : ( وكلُّ مَنْ لم يكن ثم كانَ فلهُ مُوجِدٌ أوجده ) . . للعلم بها قوله : ( لاستحالةِ أنْ تُوجِدَ نفسَكَ ) يعني : أَنَّكَ لمَّا احتجت إلى مُرجح لوجودِكَ على عدمِكَ السابق .. لزمَ أنْ يكونَ ذلكَ المرجّحُ غيركَ . قوله : ( وإلا لأمكن أن تُوجِدَ ما هو أهون عليكَ مِنْ نفسِكَ ( تقريره أن تقول : لو أمكن أن تُوجِدَ نفسَكَ .. لأمكن أن تُوجِدَ ذاتَ غيرِكَ ، والتالي باطل ، فالمقدَّم مثله بيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ القدرة على اختراع أحد المثلين قدرة على اختراع مِثْلِهِ ، والممكنات متساوية في الإمكان المصحح لتعلقِ القدرة ، فالقدرة على إيجادِ بعضها اختراعاً قدرة على إيجاد جميعها ، والتمكُنُ : التأثير ، وقوله : ( مما ) ( من ) بمعنى ( في ) ، و( ما ) واقعة على موجود ؛ أي : وكان اختيار وجوده تأثيراً في موجود ليس متصفاً بالعدم بل بالوجود ، وهذا تحصيل للحاصل .
So you see how knowledge of the temporality of the world in this method is delayed after knowledge of the existence of the Maker; thus the difference between this method and other methods has become apparent. His saying: (So you know that you have a Creator who created you) means: other than you; by the evidence that follows, and this is the result of the mentioned proof, except that he dispensed with mentioning the minor premise, which is our saying: (I was not, then I was), and omitted the major premise, which is our saying: (And everyone who was not then was has a Creator who created him)... due to knowledge of it. His saying: (due to the impossibility that you create yourself) means: that since you needed a preponderator for your existence over your prior non-existence, it follows that that preponderator must be other than you. His saying: (Otherwise it would be possible for you to create what is easier for you than yourself) — its formulation is that you say: If it were possible for you to create yourself, then it would be possible for you to create the essence of another, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the implication: That the ability to invent one of two similar things is an ability to invent its like, and possible things are equal in possibility that qualifies [them] for the attachment of ability, so the ability to invent some of them is an ability to invent all of them. And 'al-tamakkun' means effect. And His saying: (مما) — (من) is in the sense of (في), and (ما) applies to an existent; i.e., His choice of its existence was an effect in an existent not described by non-existence but by existence, and this is a realization of what is already obtained.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on a previous kalam proof. It first highlights a methodological distinction: in this proof, knowledge of the temporal origination of the world comes after knowledge of the existence of the Creator, unlike other proofs. Then it explains the proof itself: the statement 'you know that you have a Creator who created you' is a result of the proof, but the proof is presented by mentioning only the minor premise ('I was not, then I was') and omitting the major premise ('Everyone who was not then was has a Creator who created him') because it is self-evident. The impossibility of self-creation is argued: since you needed a preponderator for your existence over your prior non-existence, that preponderator must be other than you. If you could create yourself, you could create another, which is absurd. The implication is justified by the parity of possibles: ability to create one possible entails ability to create its like, since all possibles are equal in possibility. The final note clarifies that 'choice of existence' is an effect in an existent, not in non-existence, which would be redundant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it uses one's own existence to prove a Creator.
Correction: The proof is not circular; it relies on the self-evident major premise (everything that begins to exist has a cause) and the minor premise (I began to exist), which together entail a Creator distinct from oneself.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the major premise (everyone who was not then was has a Creator) was omitted?
A) Because it is self-evident and does not need mention.
B) Because it is false and should be hidden.
C) Because the minor premise alone is sufficient without it.
D) Because the proof is incomplete without it.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of self-creation is logically derived from the need for a preponderator and the parity of possible beings.

What would change in your daily life if you lived with the constant awareness that every moment you exist is a gift from your Creator, not a right? ?
The argument shows that self-creation is impossible because you would need to be before you were to cause yourself. When you think about your own desires and ambitions, do you ever act as if you are the cause of your own success or goodness? Write down one area where you have been acting as if you are self-sufficient, and determine how you can turn that into an act of gratitude to your Creator.

Repent of that illusion today.

The Two Claims in the Implication of Self-Creation
Sanusi explains the two claims in the implication and proves why creating another is easier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two claims within the implication of self-creation and why creating another is logically easier.

Core Idea
Self-creation is impossible because it forces a contradiction: the agent must both precede and be identical to itself.

Sanusi unpacks the earlier proof by distinguishing two claims hidden in a single implication. He then demonstrates that self-creation involves a logical contradiction that creating another avoids, making the second claim provable. This passage sharpens our understanding of why the argument from possibility leads to the necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the translation. Notice that Sanusi begins by clarifying the implication and then explicitly states the two claims.
The passage uses technical terms like 'ملازمة' (implication) and 'دعويان' (two claims).
2
Identify the two claims
Find where Sanusi says 'اشتملت الملازمة على دعويين' and list them: (1) whoever can create self can create another, (2) creating another is easier.
The first claim is about possibility, the second about comparative ease.
3
Understand the proof for claim 1
The proof is simple: equality in possibility (مساواتك في الإمكان). If both are possible, then self-creation implies the possibility of creating another.
4
Understand the proof for claim 2
Creating another is easier because self-creation involves a contradiction: the agent must precede itself (as agent) and be identical to its act (as product). This contradiction is absent when creating another.
Sanusi explains this as 'الجمع بين أمرين غير متنافيين' – combining two contradictory matters.
5
Reflect on the metaphor
Sanusi uses 'تهافت الفراش في النار' (moths falling into fire) to illustrate the absurdity and collapse of the self-creation scenario.
This metaphor underscores the impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى بيانِ الملازمة أشار بقولِهِ : ( لمساواتِهِ لكَ في الإمكان ) أي : لمساواة غيرِكَ لكَ في الإمكانِ وأمَّا بطلان التالي - وهو أنَّ إيجاد الإنسانِ ذاتَ غيره ممتنع - فلا يفتقر إلى بيانٍ ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ عاقل يدركُ مِنْ نفسِهِ العجز عن ذلك قولُهُ : ( وإنَّما قلنا : إِنَّهُ أهونُ عليكَ ( لمَّا اشتملَتِ الملازمة على دعويين : إحداهما : أَنَّ مَنْ أمكنَ أنْ يُوجِدَ نفسَهُ أمكنَ أَنْ يُوجِدَ غيرَهُ ، الثانيةُ : أَنَّ إيجادَهُ غيرَهُ أهون عليهِ مِنْ إيجادِهِ نفسَه .. احتاجَ إلى الاستدلال عليهما ، فاستدل على الأولى بقوله : ( لمساواتِهِ لكَ في الإمكان ) ، واحتج هنا على الثانية، فبيَّنَ أَنَّ وَجْهَ الأهونيَّةِ في إيجادِ سلامتهُ مِنْ محالٍ يختص بإيجادِهِ نفسَهُ ؛ وهو الجمعُ بينَ أمرينِ الغير متنافيين مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ يجب أن يتقدَّمَ على نفسه ؛ لكونه فاعلاً لها ، والفاعلُ قبلَ فعلِهِ ضرورةً ، ويجب تأخُرُهُ لكونه عينَ فعلِهِ ، وهو قول متهافت أي: متساقط ، ومنه : تهافت الفراش في النارِ؛ أي : تساقط
And to the explanation of the implication he pointed with his saying: (due to its equality with you in possibility), i.e., due to the equality of the other with you in possibility. As for the falsity of the consequent — which is that a human's creating the essence of another is impossible — it does not need proof, because every rational person perceives from himself inability for that. His saying: (And we only said: it is easier for you) — since the implication included two claims: one of them: that whoever could create himself could create another, the second: that his creating another is easier for him than his creating himself... he needed to provide evidence for both. So he provided evidence for the first with his saying: (due to its equality with you in possibility), and he argued here for the second, and he explained that the aspect of easiness in creating the other is freedom from a difficulty specific to creating oneself, which is combining two mutually contradictory matters: that he must precede himself, since he is the agent of it, and the agent is before his act necessarily, and he must be after himself because he is identical to his act; and this is a contradictory saying, i.e., collapsing, and from it is the falling of moths into fire, i.e., falling.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the logical steps in the earlier proof. The author clarifies that the implication (if self-creation were possible then creating another would be possible) contains two distinct claims: (1) the possibility of self-creation entails the possibility of creating another, (2) creating another is easier than creating oneself. The first claim is proven by the equality in possibility (both are possible). The second claim requires a separate proof, which the author provides: creating another is easier because it avoids the contradiction inherent in self-creation. Self-creation requires the agent to both precede itself (as agent) and be identical to its own act (as product), which is impossible. This contradiction makes self-creation impossible, so creating another is comparatively easier (merely impossible in a different way, but the point is that the impossibility of self-creation is more absurd). The term 'mutahāfit' (collapsing) is explained with the metaphor of moths falling into fire.

دعويان
أهون
إمكان
فاعل

تهافت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'easier' implies possibility, but here 'easier' refers to the logical difficulty of self-contradiction, not comparative feasibility.
Correction: Sanusi's argument shows that creating another is 'easier' only in the sense that it does not involve the logical absurdity of self-creation; both are actually impossible for humans, but self-creation is impossible in a more contradictory way.

Checkpoint

A) It requires the agent to exist before itself.
B) It is equal to creating another.
C) Human beings lack power.
D) It violates the principle of non-contradiction only indirectly.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of self-creation arises from the logical contradiction of an agent having to both precede and be identical to its own act, making creating another comparatively 'easier'.

The Three Modes of Influence: Definition and Division
Sanusi recaps the three modes of influence and defines each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three modes of influence (choice, nature, cause) and how they are distinguished based on whether the agent can refrain from its effect and whether conditions and impediments apply.

Core Idea
Every agent either can refrain from its effect or cannot, and the latter splits into nature and cause.

Sanusi now deepens our understanding of causality by formally defining the three ways an agent can influence its effect. This classification is fundamental for analyzing theological and philosophical arguments about creation and divine action. The passage uses a tight logical division based on the agent's ability to refrain and the role of conditions and impediments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from the three modes to the subdivision of agents that cannot refrain.
Pay attention to the examples: writer, fire, key.
2
Identify the three modes
Find and list the three modes of influence: influence by choice, influence by nature, influence by cause. Understand which category each belongs to in the division.
The division is based on whether the agent can refrain from its effect.
3
Understand the basis of restriction
The passage says every agent either can refrain from its effect or cannot. The first is the free agent; the second is further divided into nature and cause. Grasp the defining criteria.
Nature depends on conditions and absence of impediments; cause does not.
4
Reflect on the examples
The examples (writer, fire, key) illustrate each category. Ask yourself: Why does the writer represent free choice? Why is fire nature and the key a cause?
The example of the writer includes a note about the Qadari and Sunni views on power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقدَّمَ انحصار جهاتِ التأثير في أوجه ثلاثة ؛ وهي التأثير بالاختيار ، والتأثير بالطبيعة ، والتأثيرُ بالعِلَّةِ ، وأَنَّ وَجْهَ الحصرِ : أَنَّ كلَّ مُؤثرٍ إِمَّا أن يصح منه الترك لأثره ؛ كالكاتب مثلاً للكتابة ، والمتحرك غير المرتعش مثلاً لحركتِهِ عندَ القدري ، لا عند السُّني القائل بعدمِ تأثيرِ القدرةِ الحادثة ، أو لا ، والأوَّلُ : الفاعلُ المختارُ ، ويلزمُهُ أنْ يكونَ حيّاً عالماً قادراً مريداً ، والثاني : إما أن يَتوقَّفَ اقتضاؤُهُ على شرط وانتفاء مانع كما يقولُ الطبائعي في إحراقِ النارِ ونفع الأدوية مثلاً ، فإنَّهُ قد يمنع منها مانع ، أو لا ، كما يقولُ الفيلسوفي في حركة اليد مع حركة المفتاح مثلاً ، فإنه يستحيل أن يمنعَ من حركة المفتاح أوِ الخاتم الكائنين في اليد عند حركته مانع ، والأوّلُ : الطبيعةُ ، والثاني : العِلَّةُ
It has preceded that the modes of influence are restricted to three aspects: influence by choice, influence by nature, and influence by cause. And the basis of the restriction is that every agent either it is possible for it to refrain from its effect—like the writer, for example, from writing, and the non-trembling mover, for example, from his movement according to the Qadari, not according to the Sunni who says that the originated power has no effect—or not. The first is the free agent, and it necessarily follows that it be living, knowing, powerful, willing. The second: either its requiring (the effect) depends on a condition and absence of an impediment, as the naturalist says regarding the burning of fire and the benefit of medicines, for example, for an impediment may prevent them, or not, as the philosopher says regarding the movement of the hand with the movement of the key, for example, for it is impossible that an impediment prevent the movement of the key or the ring that are in the hand when it moves. The first is nature, and the second is cause.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the three modes of influence. It begins by stating that the modes are restricted to three: influence by choice (al-ikhtiyār), by nature (al-ṭabīʿa), and by cause (al-ʿilla). Then it explains the logical basis of this restriction: every agent either can refrain from its effect or cannot. The first is the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār), which must be living, knowing, powerful, and willing. The second (which cannot refrain) is further subdivided: either its effect depends on a condition and absence of an impediment—this is nature (al-ṭabīʿa)—or it does not and no impediment can prevent it—this is cause (al-ʿilla). Examples are given for each: the writer can refrain from writing; fire burning may be prevented by an impediment; the movement of a key with the hand is necessary and cannot be prevented. Thus the passage gives a clear definition of each term and the logical division.

Key Terms
تأثير بالاختيار
تأثير بالطبيعة
تأثير بالعلة
فاعل مختار
طبيعة
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'nature' and 'cause' are interchangeable terms for the same kind of influence.
Correction: They are distinct: nature depends on conditions and can be impeded, while cause is unconditionally necessary and cannot be prevented.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the distinguishing feature between the agent by nature and the agent by cause?
A) Nature requires will, cause does not.
B) Nature is always subject to impediments, cause is not.
C) Nature can refrain from its effect, cause cannot.
D) Nature requires a condition and absence of impediment, cause does not.

Key Takeaway
The three modes of influence—choice, nature, and cause—are distinguished by whether the agent can refrain from its effect and whether the effect depends on conditions and freedom from impediments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act, do you truly believe you could have refrained? What does this lesson's classification reveal about your own accountability before Allah for every choice you make? Will you start each day acknowledging that you are a free agent responsible for your actions?
?
Consider something you think happens 'by nature'—like the sun rising. How does this passage challenge you to rethink whether it is truly nature or cause? Are you trusting in natural patterns as if they are necessary, thereby forgetting that Allah is the ultimate agent? Will you consciously attribute effects to Allah's power today?
?
The free agent must be living, knowing, powerful, and willing. Examine your own life: where do you act out of mere habit rather than deliberate knowledge and will? What is one area where you will replace automatic reaction with intentional choice to better reflect your status as a free agent?

The Obligatory Judgment: A Self Attribute and the Tamathul Proof
Sanusi defines the wajib judgment and proves it via tamathul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligatory judgment must be a self attribute or its concomitant, and how the tamathul proof demonstrates that no two similars can differ in such a judgment.

Core Idea
An obligatory judgment is not an action; it is a self attribute of the essence.

Sanusi begins by establishing the nature of the wajib (obligatory) judgment. He argues that such a judgment is not a contingent attribute but rather inseparably belongs to the essence itself. He then provides a powerful logical proof using the concept of tamathul (likeness) to demonstrate that assigning an obligatory judgment to one of two identical things would lead to a contradiction. This lesson unpacks both the definition and the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal translation. Notice the density and logical structure.
The passage contains a definition followed by a conditional proof.
2
Understand the definition of obligatory judgment
The first sentence states that an obligatory judgment must be either a self attribute (nafsī) or a concomitant (lāzim) of a self attribute. Reflect on why it cannot be an attribute of action.
A self attribute is one that has no existence beyond the essence.
3
Follow the tamathul proof step by step
The proof assumes two similar things (mithlān). If one is specified with an obligatory judgment, that judgment would be a self attribute or its concomitant, making the two differ in self attributes. This contradicts their similarity, leading to a contradiction: a similar that is not similar.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof shows that such specification is impossible. Consider how this reinforces the definition and the nature of obligatory judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الواجبُ لا يكون إلا صفةً نفسية أو لازماً لها ] وقوله : ( لِمَا يلزمُ مِن اجتماعِ متنافيين ) هذا بيان لبطلان التالي ، وهو جواب الشرط ؛ أي : يلزم على اختصاص أحدِ المِثْلين بحكم واجب أن يكونَ مِثْلاً غيرَ مِثْلٍ ؛ يعني : لأنَّ التماثل يقتضي استواء المثلين في جميع صفاتِ النفسِ ؛ أي : الصفاتِ التي ليس لها وجود زائد على الذاتِ ، واختصاص أحدهما بحكم واجب ـ وهو لا يكونُ إلا صفةً نفسيةً أو لازماً لها - يُوجِبُ انفراد أحدهما عن نفسية ، فلا يشتركان في جميع صفاتِ النفس ، فلا يكونُ إِذا مِثْلاً له ، كيف وقد تحقَّقَ أَنَّهُ مثل له ؟! فقد لزم أن يكونَ مِثْلاً غير مثل ! وهو تهافت
The obligatory judgment is not but a self attribute or a concomitant of it. And his saying: (because of what results from the conjunction of two contradictories) this is an explanation of the invalidity of the consequent, and it is the answer of the conditional; i.e., it results from the specification of one of the two similars with an obligatory judgment that it would be a similar that is not similar; meaning: because similarity requires equality of the two similars in all self attributes; i.e., attributes that have no additional existence beyond the essence, and the specification of one of them with an obligatory judgment—and it is not but a self attribute or a concomitant of it—necessitates the distinctness of one of them from a self attribute, so they do not share in all self attributes, so it is not then similar to it, how while it has been confirmed that it is similar to it?! So it necessarily follows that it would be a similar that is not similar! And it is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of the wajib (obligatory) judgment in kalam and provides a proof from tamathul (likeness). The author states that a wajib judgment must be either a nafsī (self) attribute or a lāzim (concomitant) of such an attribute. He then explains the proof: if one of two perfectly similar things (mithlān) were singled out for a wajib judgment, that would violate their tamathul, because tamathul requires equality in all nafsī attributes. Since the wajib judgment is a nafsī attribute or its concomitant, giving it to one similar would make it distinctive, contradicting the assumption of tamathul. Therefore, the conclusion is that such a specification is impossible—it would entail a contradiction (mithl ghayr mithl). The argument follows the classical pattern of reductio ad absurdum (ilzām).

Key Terms
الحكم الواجب
صفة نفسية
لازم
تماثل
مثل
تهافت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an obligatory judgment is an attribute of action or something that can be acquired.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is a self attribute or its concomitant, meaning it is inherent to the essence and cannot be separated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what kind of attribute is an obligatory judgment?
A) An attribute of action
B) A self attribute (nafsī) or its concomitant
C) A relational attribute
D) A contingent attribute

Key Takeaway
The obligatory judgment is inherently tied to the essence, and the tamathul proof shows that assigning it to one of two identical things is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The tamathul proof shows that if two essences are identical, they cannot differ in any self attribute. Do you truly believe that no one shares even one of Allah's essential attributes? Take a moment to identify a subtle way you might associate something with Allah that belongs only to Him, and commit to eliminating that from your heart today.
?
Sanusi defines the wajib judgment as a self attribute, not an action. When you fulfill your obligations to Allah, do you see them as flowing from your essential nature as a servant, or as mere tasks? What would change if you lived each obligation as an expression of who you are? Write one specific change you will make this week.
?
The proof ends in a contradiction if one assumes difference among similars. Are there contradictions in your beliefs about Allah that you haven’t resolved? Choose one such contradiction you have allowed to persist (e.g., believing He is Merciful yet feeling He is harsh). How will you reconcile that today with the truth from this lesson?

Objection and the Mutakallimun's Division of Existence
Sanusi presents an objection and the mutakallimun's division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the proof of contingency only applies to substances and accidents, and the mutakallimun's attempt to confine the world to these categories through a division of existents.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun argue that all existence is either substance or accident.

Sanusi begins by raising a critical objection: the proof of contingency only shows that substances and accidents are originated. But we need to prove that everything besides God is contingent. How can we be sure there isn't something else? The mutakallimun answer by stating that the world is confined to substances and accidents, and they support this with a famous division of existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part where Sanusi presents the objection. Notice that the objection says the proof only covers substances and accidents, but the required conclusion is broader.
The objector questions the scope of the proof.
2
Identify the mutakallimun's response
Sanusi then reports the mutakallimun's position: the world consists only of substances and accidents. They have several ways to disprove anything beyond, all weak.
The main response is the division (taqseem).
3
Study the division of existents
The division: every existent is either spatially located (substance) or not. Non-spatially located either subsists in something (accident) or neither (God). Trace this classification.
This is an attempt to show no other category exists.
4
Reflect on the intended conclusion
Ask yourself: does this division successfully prove that everything besides God is either substance or accident? Why might it fail?
The passage hints at weakness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل ينحصرُ العالم في الجواهر والأعراض ؟ حدوث فإن قلت: برهانُكُمُ السابق والآتي بعدُ إِنَّما يُنتجان الحدوثَ لجميع الجواهر وأعراضها ، والمطلوب أعمُّ مِنْ هذا ؛ وهو كل ما سوى الله تعالى ، فلو قُدِّرَ فيما سواه جلَّ وعلا ما ليس بجرم ولا قائماً به . . لم ينهض فيه دليلكم قلتَ : مذهب المتكلمينَ انحصارُ العالم في الجواهر وأعراضها ، ولهم في إبطال الزائدِ طرقٌ كلُّها ضعيفةٌ ، من أشهرها طريق التقسيم ؛ قالوا : كل موجود إما أن يكونَ مُتحيّزاً أو غير متحيز ، وغير المتحيز إما أن يقوم بمُتحيّز أو لا ؛ فالمُتحيّرُ : هو الجوهر ، والقائم به : هو العرض، وما ليس بمُتحيّر ولا قائم بمُتحيّر : هو الله جل وعلا وصفات ذاتِهِ فهذهِ القسمة وإنْ كانَتْ دائرةً بين النفي والإثباتِ . . ضعيفةٌ ؛ لأنَّ ما انتهى إليه التقسيم - وهو ما ليس بمُتحيّز ولا قائم بمتحيز - ليسَ نفس حقيقتِهِ جلَّ وعلا ولا نفسَ حقيقةِ صفاتِ ذاتِهِ ، فللخصم أن يمنع تخصيصه بهما ، فلا تفيدُ القسمة المطلوب ، والذي اختاره بعضُ مُحققي المتأخّرينَ في هذه المسألةِ : الوقف في وجودِ هذا الزائد ) ، وهو الظاهرُ عندي حامدي » (ص (١٢٤) ، وقد يشهد لهذا الظاهر قوله سبحانه : ﴿ وَلَكِن لَّا نَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ ﴾ [الإسراء : ٤٤] إذ الدلالة على تعظيم المولى مُدركة للمتأمل .
[Does the world consist only of substances and accidents?] Origination. If you say: Your previous and subsequent proof only produces origination for all substances and their accidents, and the required is more general than this; it is everything other than God Almighty. So if it is supposed that among what is other than Him, there is something that is neither a body nor subsistent in it, your proof would not apply. I say: The doctrine of the mutakallimun is that the world is confined to substances and their accidents. They have ways to invalidate the extra, all of which are weak. The most famous is the way of division (taqseem): They said: Every existent is either spatially located (mutahayyiz) or not spatially located. What is not spatially located either subsists in something spatially located or not. So the spatially located is the substance (jawhar), what subsists in it is the accident (arad), and what is not spatially located and does not subsist in what is spatially located is God Almighty and the attributes of His essence. So this division, even though it revolves between negation and affirmation, is weak because what the division ends at—i.e., what is not spatially located and does not subsist in what is spatially located—is not the very reality of God Almighty nor the very reality of the attributes of His essence. So the opponent can deny its specification to them. So the division does not yield the required. And the choice of some later investigators in this matter is: stopping (waqf) regarding the existence of this extra (i.e., what is beyond substance and accident), and it is apparent to me. And what may support this apparent is His saying: 'But you do not comprehend their glorification' [al-Isra: 44], since the indication of glorifying the Lord is perceived by the contemplator.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection states that the previous proof of the world's contingency (huduth) only proves that substances and their accidents are originated, but the required conclusion is that everything other than God is originated. If there were something that is neither a substance nor an accident, the proof would not cover it. The answer begins by stating the position of the mutakallimun: the world is limited to substances and accidents. They have several ways to prove that there is no other category, all weak. The most famous is the method of division (taqseem): they divide existents into spatially located (mutahayyiz) which is substance, and non-spatially located, which either subsists in something (accident) or neither (God). But this division is weak because what results from the division is not exactly the same as the reality of God and His attributes. The opponent could object that the division does not necessarily apply only to God. Therefore, the author mentions that some later investigators choose to stop (waqf) regarding the existence of something beyond substance and accident, and the author finds this apparent. He supports it with the verse about glorification, implying that the existence of other entities is possible but we cannot be certain.

Key Terms
جوهر (substance)
عرض (accident)
تقسيم (division)
متحيز (spatially located)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the proof of contingency directly applies to all existents without needing to first confine the world to substances and accidents.
Correction: The passage shows that the mutakallimun must first argue that the world consists only of substances and accidents; otherwise, the proof's scope is insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the doctrine of the mutakallimun regarding the composition of the world?
A) The world includes substances, accidents, and other entities.
B) The world is confined to substances and their accidents.
C) The world consists only of accidents.
D) The world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun attempt to limit the world to substances and accidents via a division, but Sanusi considers this division weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical argument for your faith that seems neat and tidy, do you ever resist hearing that it is actually weak? Can you recall a time you clung to a flawed proof because it was comfortable? What would it cost you to let it go?
?
Sanusi calls the mutakallimun's division weak, yet they presented it as authoritative. Where in your own life do you present arguments or beliefs that you haven't fully examined, assuming they are stronger than they truly are? Will you commit to testing one such belief today?
?
The objector in this passage raises a valid point that challenges the standard proof. Are you willing to honestly confront objections to your theological convictions, or do you dismiss them without hearing them? How might God be calling you to a deeper, more rigorous faith through such challenges?

The Weakness of the Division and the Position of Waqf
Sanusi explains the weakness of the division and the choice of waqf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the division of existents is considered weak and the position of some later investigators who choose to stop (waqf) regarding the existence of entities beyond substance and accident.

Core Idea
The division is weak; the safer position is to suspend judgment.

Sanusi now explains why the mutakallimun's division is weak: the final category does not match God's reality exactly, so opponents can reject its restriction to God. Consequently, some later scholars adopt a position of waqf (suspension) regarding whether any entity exists beyond substance and accident. Sanusi finds this apparent and supports it with a Qur'anic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of the division
Read the part where Sanusi explains why the division is weak: the result is not identical to God's essence, so the opponent can deny its applicability.
The weakness lies in the mismatch between the division's endpoint and its intended referent.
2
Understand the position of waqf
Sanusi reports that some later investigators choose to stop (waqf) regarding the existence of something beyond substance and accident. He says this is apparent to him.
Waqf means suspending judgment, neither affirming nor denying.
3
Consider the supporting evidence
Sanusi cites the verse 'But you do not comprehend their glorification' to suggest that there may be entities we cannot fully grasp, supporting the choice of waqf.
This verse implies that other beings exist whose nature we cannot perceive.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?
